be traced back to the early stages of Soviet colonisation of the Arctic,
which laid the foundation for Russia's current policy in the region.

[This paper examines]{.underline} **[the development of a network of
polar meteorological stations as both an instrument of settler
colonialism]{.underline}** [and a mechanism of meteorological and
territorial governance during Soviet Russia's expansion into the Arctic
in the 1920s--30s.]{.underline} It demonstrates how the experiences of
Soviet polar scientists, shaped by interactions with various geological
and environmental forces, particularly wind and weathering, influenced
Arctic policy concerning the governance of both mineral resources and
Indigenous populations. This period is crucial for understanding modern
Russia's Arctic doctrine, a framework of ideas that emerged at that time
and has continued to inform Arctic policy today.

In line with this doctrine, in the 1950s, the Soviet Commission for
Northern Problems formalised an administrative zoning system based on
climatic and economic typologies, building on regional classifications
that had begun in the 1930s (Pilyasov et al., 2022).

[Grounded in a territorial development model that prioritised local
economic-geographical processes primarily for their contribution to
national economic expansion]{.underline}, **[this doctrine emphasised
resource extraction, population settlement, and large-scale engineering
projects designed to mitigate harsh climatic conditions]{.underline}**
(Josephson, 2014; Pilyasov et al., 2022). **[It also promoted the
so-called 'cultural development' of Indigenous peoples, who were
perceived as best adapted to the region's climate, though in practice,
many Soviet policies disrupted traditional Indigenous lifeways.
Meteorological stations]{.underline}**, [a key instrument of this
territorial policy, were not only essential for Arctic governance and
environmental monitoring but also]{.underline} **[played a crucial role
in asserting Soviet territorial sovereignty]{.underline}**. **[By
extending]{.underline}** [control beyond land into atmospheric and
volumetric dimensions---what Li (2024) terms **'sovereignty over
volume'**]{.underline}---**[these stations also became an essential
component of Soviet settler colonial policies in the
north.]{.underline}** The Soviet authorities themselves referred to
their policies as 'colonisation,' reinforcing the argument that they can
be understood primarily as a form of settler colonialism (see
Korobeinikov, 2024).

Building on broader conceptualisations of governance as the process of
directing society and the economy through collective action in pursuit
of shared objectives (Torfing et al., 2012[), this paper discusses
'meteorological governance' as the ways in which states, scientific
institutions, and other actors regulate, manage, and utilise
meteorological knowledge, infrastructure, and policies to achieve
specific political aims.]{.underline} There has been growing interest in
meteorological/climate governance during the last two decades, driven by
concerns over climate change and associated need for global monitoring,
as well as the technical infrastructure needed to buttress the emerging
structures and policy-making (e.g. Edwards, 2024; Tosun & Schoenefeld,
2017). In Russia, meteorological governance has long been integral to
the Arctic doctrine and a defining feature of the Soviet meteorological
project more broadly (see Oldfield, 2018; Oldfield & Poberezhskaya,
2023; Oldfield & Shaw, 2015). This paper examines Arctic meteorological
governance, focusing on meteorological stations as instruments of
settler colonial policies. Although McCannon (1998) has highlighted the
role of weather stations in disseminating Soviet culture in the north,
little research has examined their influence in shaping Arctic frontiers
and governing human populations in the region.

[**The role of 'weather infrastructure' as a symbol of colonial power**
has recently been emphasised in research by Mona Bhan (2023). **The use
of meteorological facilities, including weather stations, as sites for
constructing colonial narratives and imaginaries**]{.underline} [has
also been explored in studies on the relationship between meteorological
sciences and colonialism]{.underline} (see Feklova, 2019; JankoviÂ´ c,
2015; Lehmann, 2018; Li, 2024; Mahony, 2024; Mahony & Endfield, 2018;
Mahony & Randalls, 2020). [However, this framing of science and
colonialism as a dualistic relationship often overlooks the multilinear
dynamics through which **scientific practices and colonial structures
mutually shaped one another**]{.underline}. In addition, while much of
the existing research has explored the use of meteorological facilities
within settler colonial systems in the West, particularly in former
European colonies, there remains a gap in research on the Soviet
socialist system. This system had a distinct understanding of
'Indigeneity' and the relationship between human populations and
territories, particularly in the context of mineral resource management.
In Soviet ideology, all citizens, including Indigenous populations, were
metaphorically and politically integrated into the 'geo-body' of the
nation, where the collective body of the state was not only defined by
territorial boundaries but also encompassed its people as part of
geological productive forces (Mamontova, 2023). This understanding
contrasts with Western settler-colonial frameworks and highlights the
need for further investigation into the role of meteorological
governance in Soviet territorial integration and cultural assimilation,
and the lasting legacies of these processes. [This paper shows
that]{.underline} '**[conquering' the Arctic entailed not only the
physical occupation of space but also the task of understanding and
managing its climate as a strategic tool for extending the geographical
reach of Soviet political power within the Arctic itself and across the
broader Soviet Union]{.underline}**. **[This objective was set as early
as the 1920s, and Indigenous people were viewed as instrumental within
this policy.]{.underline}**

This paper is theoretically informed by emerging research on 'colonial
atmospheres,' focusing on the conceptualisation of weathering as a
socio-political concept. Two theoretical approaches primarily inform
this research: the colonial dimensions of meteorological governance (e.
g., Bhan, 2023; Collins, 2020, 2024; Dalby, 2013; Ghertner, 2021; Legg,
2020; Li, 2024; McCormack, 2008) and phenomenology-grounded
understandings of weathering as an embodied experience, particularly
those developed by anthropologist Tim Ingold (2007). The former approach
reflects the shift in political geography from focusing solely on
territories to incorporating volumes into research (Elden, 2013) to
examine how **[colonial power dynamics influence social hierarchies and
the marginalisation of Indigenous and local communities through
meteorological governance]{.underline}**. Central to this governance was
the Soviet state's 'vertical geopolitics' (Graham, 2004), which
encompassed the Arctic atmosphere, subterranean mineral resources,
oceanic waters, polar air, and drifting ice.

[**Building on Stephen Legg's (2020) discussion of 'white atmospheres, '
this paper further contrasts the experiences of polar scientists with
the racialised concept of the 'weather-resistant Indigenous'---a
colonial trope that framed Arctic Indigenous populations as inherently
more resilient and adaptable to extreme climates than metropolitan
scientists, which was often used to place them in a position where their
labour, knowledge, and resilience could be extracted for colonial
purposes.** This notion is used to examine the 'racialised geographical
imaginations' (Legg, 2020, p. 778) employed by Soviet authorities, who
positioned Indigenous adaptability to the Arctic climate as a mechanism
of settler colonialism]{.underline}. **[Yet, whereas in a capitalist
system, colonial adaptation narratives construct Indigenous peoples as
'vulnerable others' expected to become 'resilient' to climate
change]{.underline}** (Islam et al., 2024, p. 7), **[the socialist
system produced 'valuable others,' whose climate resilience was treated
as an asset to be controlled and directed toward national economic
development. Soviet authorities exploited this perceived resilience,
which was based on Indigenous Arctic experiences, to advance the
colonisation of remote polar islands by both relocating Indigenous
populations from mainland Russia to previously uninhabited islands and
sedentarising nomadic groups into permanent communities.]{.underline}**

This exploitation of Indigenous resilience can be understood as a form
of weathering in the sense proposed by Yolanda Collins (2024),
encompassing not only the physical endurance of harsh conditions but
also the socio-political transformations and shifting relationships with
ancestral lands under colonial governance and racialised imaginaries.
[Collins calls for a shift away from land-centred perspectives to
examine how **political power operates in and through the
air.**]{.underline} She develops the political ecology of atmospheres,
referring 'not only to crisis and capital, but to material and symbolic
entities that emerge from socio-political, economic, and biophysical
processes across scales and time frames' (Collins, 2024, p. 4).
[**Collins interprets weathering as a process that generates weather
patterns, encompassing human-induced weathering through extractive
activities that accelerate climatic changes.** She uses the concept of
weathering as an analytical tool to engage with slow socio-political
processes, through which the notion of 'colonial atmospheres'
emerges.]{.underline} This understanding of weathering as disposition
can be problematised and expanded to account for nomadic populations
whose dynamic and fluid relationships with territories, including
subterranean spaces, challenge fixed spatial conceptualisations and
complicate analysing settler-colonial policies. I use the term
'weathering Indigenous' in the title to encompass this complexity when
nomadic lifestyles are transformed into settled populations,
highlighting the coloniality of this reversed order.

Exploring the fluidity in the volumetric understanding of territory,
Steinberg and Peters (2015) highlight how the vertical dimension of
volume is often dematerialised, reinforcing a static, grounded
conception of matter. This critique is particularly evident in
solid-fluid territories, such as marine environments, where the fluidity
of territorial boundaries challenges fixed notions of territoriality
(e.g., Steinberg & Peters, 2015; Wang, 2023). Building on this critique
and following Marston and Himley's (2021) definition of territory, this
paper conceptualises the Arctic as fundamentally fluid---characterised
not only by shifting boundaries of ice, water, and geological formations
but also by the dynamic interplay of politics and cultural practices
that shape its imagination, perception, and governance. This paper
argues that **[the Arctic's fluid and unstable terrain, marked by
permeable depths, oscillating ground, persistent weathering, and
undulating horizons, was perceived by Soviet authorities and polar
scientists as both a barrier to colonisation and a space that required
Indigenous knowledge for territorial control. Soviet authorities sought
to frame Indigenous peoples as predominantly fixed and stable, rather
than as obstacles to be eradicated, in alignment with their aspiration
for a 'fixed' Arctic---one in which both the environment and its peoples
were manageable and predictable]{.underline}**. This perception
distinguished Soviet settler colonialism in the Arctic from other
settler colonial strategies, which are commonly based on the principle
of unbounded territoriality. Hughes (2020, p. 217) describes this as a
'strategy of territorial control best exercised by not delimiting
boundaries, by not making clear the extent of sovereign authority.'
**[Such strategies typically involve the erasure or assimilation of
Indigenous minorities.]{.underline}**

As primary research material for this paper, I use selected published
works by Soviet polar scientists, foremost Boris Ostrovsky, who were
involved in the construction of the first Soviet polar stations in the
Arctic in the 1920s and 1930s. Ostrovsky documented nuanced details of
work in the Arctic. Recent research explores relational experiences in
isolated conditions, such as polar stations and outer space (e.g.,
Battaglia et al., 2012; Mandel, 2021; Olson, 2018; Pottier, 2022). The
analysis of these experiences opens up a discussion on radically
different atmospheric ecologies in terms of time-space routine and
human-environmental relations. Soviet scientists' diaries and reports
serve as self-ethnographies, offering insights into environmental
adaptation in the high north. These sources also shed light on the
livelihoods of Arctic Indigenous communities and the relations between
them and polar scientists. Materials related to the Soviet settler
colonisation of the Arctic and Indigenous people were collected in the
State Archive of the Russian Federation (GARF) in Moscow.

#### Settler adaptation perpetuates colonialism by using Indigenous people in the Arctic to figure out the ideal conditions of their land later to extract the materials, instrumentalizing them for geopolitical purposes. 

**Mamontova**, Newtonian International Fellow at University of
Birmingham -- School of Geography, Earth and Environmental Sciences,
**2025**

\[Nadezhda, "Governing the unstable: Colonial atmospheres, 'weathering'
Indigenous,and the colonisation of polar air in the Soviet Arctic",
https://doi.org/10.1016/j.polgeo.2025.103331, pgs. 5-6 accessed July 9
2025 -- HDCSW\]

2.3. 'Weathering' Indigenous

[Before Soviet colonisation of the Arctic, most of the islands remained
uninhabited due to their harsh climatic conditions. However, some, like
Novaya Zemlya and Vaygach Island, were seasonally visited by Indigenous
populations as part of their annual nomadic cycles.]{.underline} In
additon, a few Nenets families had been living permanently in Novaya
Zemlya since at least the nineteenth century. These nomadic pastoralist
communities, including the Nenets, Even, Tundra Chukchi, Koryak,
Yukaghir, Tundra Enets, and Nganasan, along with the semi-nomadic marine
hunting groups such as the Chukchi, Asiatic Yupik, and Aleut, were
officially recognised as Indigenous under the Soviet nationality policy
of the 1920s. These communities represented a special interest for the
Soviet state during its industrialisation campaign in the north. **[As
Yuri Slezkine (1994, p. 138) emphasises, they were key to the 'economic
development of almost one-third of the country's territory.' The Arctic
is a region of enormous animal and mineral wealth, yet only the
well-adapted Indigenous populations were considered capable of fully
exploiting these resources. If these native communities were to
disappear, this potentially prosperous land would risk becoming nothing
more than a 'frozen wasteland']{.underline}** (Slezkine, 1994, p. 138).
The task of support and preservation of Indigenous people was identified
by the Supreme Council for National Economy as 'a matter of great
national importance' (1925, GARF, P3977-2-30; see also Mamontova, 2023).

As the Soviet government worked to establish permanent populations on
the islands to assert its claims over these Arctic territories,
[Indigenous people were particularly recognised for their remarkable
adaptability to local weather conditions.]{.underline} [Their endurance
and resilience in dealing with extreme weather conditions, in tandem
with exceptional navigation skills, were appreciated by polar
scientists]{.underline}. **[Significant attention was given to
Indigenous weather knowledge systems, specifically those of the Chukchi
and Yupik peoples in the Arctic]{.underline}** (see Bogoslavskaya &
Krupnik, 2013; Kolomiets & Krupnik, 2020; Krupnik et al., 2010).
Indigenous community members made substantial contributions to
understanding and predicting weather, sea ice behaviour, and currents in
the Arctic Ocean, based on their profound navigation skills in open
waters and deep knowledge of the migrations of birds and sea mammals.
These skills formed the body of traditional Indigenous knowledge, which
can be broadly defined as 'a network of knowledges, beliefs, and
traditions intended to preserve, communicate, and contextualize
Indigenous relationships with culture and landscape over time' (Bruchac,
2014, p. 3814). During the early Soviet period of colonisation, due to
these abilities, **[Indigenous community members were considered
instrumental, and Soviet authorities were inclined to exploit their
adaptive abilities to navigate changing weather patterns for their own
benefit.]{.underline}**

The Indigenous people's techniques for moving through the unfamiliar
surroundings of the Arctic tundra during adverse weather conditions were
reported as particularly valuable. Indeed, the nomadic lifestyles of
hunting and reindeer herding communities require a combination of
detailed geographic knowledge and expert navigation skills to adapt to
various environmental challenges. For example, in Inuit geography,
horizons and geographical features are oriented using wind rather than
cardinal points (Aporta, 2005). Similarly, among the Nenets, a variety
of navigational methods are employed, including cardinal directions,
based on observations of wind speed and direction, snow cover, shapes of
plants, and animal activities (Adaev, 2018). The head of the expedition
in the Gyda tundra, B. N. Gorodkov, noted that the landscape of the
tundra was rather monotonous for an inexperienced traveller and to avoid
getting lost, 'one must either be a Samoyed or constantly keep an eye on
the compass' (Tobolyakov, 1931, p. 52). His Nenets guide and translator,
the shaman Pron'ka Nenyang (Komarov), skilfully utilised sastrugi
\[irregular ridges of hard snow formed by wind erosion\] as landmarks.
In his field diary, Gorodkov reported that 'due to the prevailing winds,
they \[sastrugi\], like a compass arrow, were almost always oriented in
the same direction in the tundra. Pron'ka noted the angle at which he
set off and then did not change this angle. In addition, he observed the
bushes, pitifully protruding from under the snow. On the leeside,
icicles hung from the bushes, and similar to snow drifts, they helped
him to maintain his direction' (Tobolyakov, 1930, p. 21). Where Russian
scholars struggled with the wind, Indigenous guides, drawing from their
knowledge and experience, were believed to know how to effectively deal
with diverse climatic conditions. This was well summarised by
topographer and geographer Vladimir Arsenyev in his famous book about
his long-term relationship with the Nanai guide Dersu Uzala: 'In
relation to a person, nature is unforgiving. After a brief caress, it
suddenly attacks, almost intentionally revealing his \[the person's\]
helplessness. The traveller constantly has to deal with the natural
phenomena, such as rain, wind, flooding, mosquitoes, swamps, cold, snow,
etc. Furthermore, the forest itself is an environmental force. Dersu, in
accordance with his surroundings, was more in tune \[with these
conditions\] than us' (Arsenyev, \[1923\] 2010, p. 104).

**[The Soviet polar scientists' reliance on the weather-wise abilities
of Indigenous communities can be understood as part of a racialised
colonial logic, where Indigenous peoples were both idealised for their
perceived connection to the environment and instrumentalised for the
state's geopolitical objectives]{.underline}** (cf. Livingstone, 2018).
Simultaneously, this perception arose from the polar scientists'
difficulties in fully adapting to the Arctic environment and their
recognition of the Indigenous peoples' expertise and mastery. Thus, in
his report, the head of the Northern Hydrographic Expedition,
Matusevich, noted that the significance of the meteorological station at
Matochkin Shar had been confirmed 'as a centre of colonisation, towards
which the sparse population, attracted to the eastern side of Novaya
Zemlya by fishing, would inevitably gravitate' (1925, GARF,
Ð 3977--1--50, p. 2). Matusevich used the term 'gravitation' to describe
the Nenets' interest in polar stations as potential hubs for trade and
exchange. After the destruction of trading posts established during the
Tsarist era, the supply issue in the tundra became critical. **[Polar
stations became a means of settler-colonisation, addressing these
critical supply challenges in the Arctic.]{.underline}** Matusevich
pointed out that all winter camps of the Nenets people were located on
the western shore of Novaya Zemlya, meaning that their nomadic trails to
the eastern shore did not often pass through the station. He concluded
that this factor needed to be considered when establishing new stations
to address the issue of the colonisation of Novaya Zemlya and
surrounding areas (1925, GARF, Ð 3977--1--50, p. 2). Evidently, the focus
on Indigenous communities near the station was connected to the need to
establish a lasting human population in this vast archipelago, with
Indigenous people seen as particularly suitable due to their
environmental knowledge and adaptability.

#### Indigenous bodies are used for territorial extraction of the Arctic -- that's the only way they are recognized; their bodies are used as a way to determine what they can extract geographically and then displacing Indigenous them for to gain political control over the Arctic. 

**Mamontova**, Newtonian International Fellow at University of
Birmingham -- School of Geography, Earth and Environmental Sciences,
**2025**

\[Nadezhda, "Governing the unstable: Colonial atmospheres, 'weathering'
Indigenous,and the colonisation of polar air in the Soviet Arctic",
https://doi.org/10.1016/j.polgeo.2025.103331, accessed July 9 2025 --
HDCSW\]

5\. Conclusion As Mahony and Endfield (2018) argue, **[imperial
ambitions have long been entangled with climate---its perception,
control, and governance. In settler-colonial contexts, meteorological
governance functions as a crucial tool for territorialisation,
population control, and resource extraction]{.underline}** (Mercer &
Simpson, 2023). [This paper demonstrates that in the Soviet Arctic,
meteorological stations were not merely sites for collecting
climate-related data; they were central to the Soviet settler colonial
project in the region.]{.underline} **[Acting as the 'eyes and ears' of
the state, these stations facilitated both environmental and
socio-political surveillance,]{.underline}** consolidating Soviet
frontiers and embedding state authority in sparsely populated areas.
**[These policies were deeply intertwined with resource extraction,
Indigenous policies, and the assertion of political control over the
Arctic atmosphere itself]{.underline}**. Controlled from Moscow,
meteorological stations played a key role in regulating the Soviet
Union's resource-based economy, thereby extending state power across
vast territories. [Beyond their economic and geopolitical functions,
they also contributed to shaping the 'polar imagination' of Soviet
citizens---an imagination built around narratives of conquest,
endurance, and the struggle against nature.]{.underline}

**[In advancing its territorial ambitions, the Soviet state valued
Indigenous peoples for their perceived adaptability to the Arctic's
harsh conditions and romanticised them as naturally suited to extreme
climates]{.underline}**. **[Interactions between polar scientists,
Indigenous communities, and the volatile Arctic environment influenced
Soviet policies, wherein Indigenous 'physical and cultural
traits'---interpreted through the racialised geographical imaginations
of polar scientists (Legg, 2020)--- were exploited to justify Indigenous
participation in Soviet colonial expansion.]{.underline}**

While meteorological governance has historically been used to exploit
Indigenous populations in various colonial contexts (e.g., JankoviÂ´ c,
2015; Livingstone, 2002), Soviet meteorological stations played a
distinct role as gravitational points for Indigenous communities.
Positioned strategically along nomadic routes, these stations helped
fill the void left by the absence of tsarist trading posts, acting as
fixed points within a fluid landscape. They facilitated trade and
exchange, reinforcing Soviet presence and subtly advancing colonial
agendas and assimilationist policies. Over time, these stations evolved
into instruments of geopolitical control, asserting volumetric
territorial sovereignty and integrating Indigenous populations into
state administrative, political, and economic structures in the north,
where they were increasingly regarded as 'valuable elements' of the
state apparatus. Indigenous knowledge of climate adaptation, developed
over generations, was framed as an essential asset within Soviet
colonisation efforts.

In contrast to other settler-colonial contexts, where Indigenous peoples
were progressively erased or displaced (Hughes, 2020; Velednitsky et
al., 2020), Soviet authorities classified Indigenous peoples as
'colonists' alongside non-Indigenous settlers, which set Soviet Arctic
governance apart. This classification was not merely rhetorical; it
functioned as a mechanism for incorporating Indigenous communities into
the Soviet modernist project while reinforcing state control over
northern populations and territories. The concept of 'Indigenous' in the
Soviet Union was territorially bound to the northward expansion of the
Russian state and recognised mainly Arctic and Siberian peoples as
Indigenous, while excluding most ethnic minorities in European Russia
and Central Asia---despite their historical territorial ties
(Stammler-Grossmann, 2009). This territorialised approach to
Indigeneity, deeply embedded in Soviet policies, continues to shape
contemporary Russian Indigenous policies.

Within the framework of meteorological governance, Soviet colonial
policies evolved toward large-scale acclimatisation efforts, exemplified
by the 1930s mass acclimatisation projects in the north (Arzyutov,
2023). With the Arctic's sparse population, state authorities sought a
workforce suited to extreme conditions, positioning Indigenous peoples,
seen as 'naturally suited' to the harsh climate, as central agents of
Soviet territorial consolidation. Over time, this objective expanded
beyond Indigenous labour to include forced Gulag labour and university
graduate distribution policies (Josephson, 2014). Alongside these
settler-colonial strategies, the Soviet state also pursued more
ambitious political goals, seeking to not only acclimate settlers to the
Arctic but to transform the polar atmosphere itself, making it more
hospitable for future generations of settler colonists. This vision of
the Arctic as a space to be mastered and controlled has been promoted
throughout the Soviet era and continues to inform Russian Arctic policy
today.

**[The perpetual instability of the Arctic atmosphere influenced Soviet
approaches to Indigenous nomadic populations, whose mobility
increasingly came to be seen as a challenge to state control in remote
areas. Rather than removing nomadic groups, Soviet authorities sought to
'fix' them in place, aligning with broader efforts to 'stabilise' the
Arctic itself through environmental interventions]{.underline}**. This
governance strategy was integral to the wider sedentarisation policies
aimed at transforming Indigenous livelihoods to fit the Soviet vision of
fixed, governable frontiers. Rather than viewing the Arctic as a
volatile frontier, this policy sought to render both its landscapes and
inhabitants predictable and controllable. Although technological
advancements reduced Soviet dependence on Indigenous knowledge, the
ideological framing of Indigenous communities as uniquely adapted to
Arctic life persisted. This racialised image remains a key element in
post-Soviet Arctic policy, particularly in relation to contemporary
Russian approaches to Indigenous peoples and their perceived
adaptability to climate change.

### Link -- Soft left -- Land Rights

#### Neoliberal hope link -- the affirmative's granting of land rights to indigenous people only opens the floodgates for neoliberal market logics that increase land extraction -- the Arctic is a hot spot for this. 

**Lindroth and Sinevaara-Niskanen**, Lindroth is a researcher of
critical studies in the Arctic at the University of Lapland --
Sinevaara-Niskanen has a PhD and is a Senior Lecturer at the University
of Lapland for Critical Arctic Studies**, 2022**

\[Marjo and Heidi, Ch. 5 Colonialism in the grammar of hope, in The
Colonial Politics of Hope: Critical Junctures of Indigenous--State
Relations, 2022, Routledge,
https://www.routledge.com/The-Colonial-Politics-of-Hope-Critical-Junctures-of-Indigenous-State-Relations/Lindroth-Sinevaara-Niskanen/p/book/9780367755676?srsltid=AfmBOor70ZCeUrnPqUC7ktaPDvqZ4AUdI9jD2gquQWJshJEt3rlcAq6Z,
pgs. 105-111, HDCSW -- Accessed July 15 2025\]

Neoliberal hope

There is a large body of critical scholarship that has demonstrated the
ways in which neoliberal rationality has insinuated itself in all
spheres of life. [Neoliberal market logic has come to prevail, and
economic parameters have entered discussions from global development to
education and personal health. **With neoliberalism, values such as
competition, capacity-building, privatisation and responsibilisation
have become normalised**]{.underline}. The focus is not only on strictly
monetary profit, but on managing an entire society through these values
(e.g. Larner and Walters 2004; Walters 2012; Brown 2015). As part of the
neoliberal move, the "private" has gained an increasing role while the
"public" has lost much of its relevance; this has seen the state
withdrawing from its previous responsibilities to provide security and
well-being. It is now up to the active individual to secure his or her
life and to be "entrepreneurial" (e.g. Agrawal 2005; Chandler and Reid
2016; Larner 2005; Li 2007, 2014).

**[Indigenous--state relations have not been spared neoliberal
rationality]{.underline}** (e.g. Howard-Wagner, Bargh and
Altamirano-JimÃ©nez 2018). On the contrary, to a growing extent these
relations have become defined by a market logic of profitability and
cost-effectiveness[**. Generally speaking, the recent willingness of
states to start drafting contracts with indigenous peoples can be
attributed to the states' interests in natural resources.**
Historically, as often today, indigenous peoples have lived in
territories with abundant resources. The increasing scarcity of certain
natural resources and the opportunities provided by climate change have
only heightened the external pressure to extract resources from
indigenous lands.]{.underline} **[The shrinking Arctic ice cap, the
scramble for accessible resources and the challenging position of Arctic
indigenous peoples combine to form a case in point]{.underline}** (e.g.
Guerrieri 2019; Nuttall 2017; Sejersen 2020).

**[Although states' efforts to meet indigenous peoples halfway might at
a first glance appear to be a sincere interest in promoting the people's
rights, their ultimate motive in doing so lies elsewhere.]{.underline}**
A recent strand of critical scholarship has pointed out how the
processes of negotiation and contractuality are driven by neoliberal
parameters that deeply affect state policies and preferences. For
example, Ronald Niezen (2017), in studying the changes in the political
climate of **[Canada,]{.underline}** points to the clash of expectations
and reality. He notes how the **[government's shift towards more
indigenous-friendly politics aroused expectations of "sweeping
reforms]{.underline}**" (Niezen 2017, 162). In his critical account, he
argues that these expectations were highly unfounded, reaching the
following conclusion:

The extent to which the government of a country with a national economy
based largely on extractive industries could rapidly and thoroughly
overhaul its policies toward indigenous peoples should probably been in
doubt from the outset. (Niezen 2017, 162)

Indeed, **[despite the symbolic and rhetorical changes in state
practices, the marketand profit-driven underpinning of indigenous--state
relations remains unchanged.]{.underline}**

In another study on Canada, [Emilie Cameron (2015) problematises the
land claim agreement processes between the state and indigenous peoples.
Her book Far Off Metal River: Inuit Lands, Settler Stories, and the
Making of the Contemporary Arctic (2015) delves into the economic and
colonial exploitation of indigenous lands. **She rejects the assumption
that land claim agreements are the solution to
decolonisation**.]{.underline} **[As she puts it, these agreements are
but tools through which the state operationalises its agenda. While
indigenous peoples are parties to these negotiations, they are the only
parties having to make trade-offs.]{.underline}** In Cameron's (2015,
18) **[words, "the agreements require Indigenous signatories to formally
cede their claims to the land in exchange for specific types of
compensation". The idea here, one according with the neoliberal logic of
cost-effectiveness, is that past injustices will be dealt with,
corrected and compensated only once. Indigenous peoples are thus assumed
to be able to put a "price tag" on their loss and
dispossession.]{.underline}** As Colin Samson and Elizabeth Cassell
note, when it comes to land rights and compensation, indigenous peoples
often "are hardly in a commanding position to question a land claims
agreement, especially when that agreement promises them immediate,
albeit temporary, cash relief" (Samson and Cassell 2013, 51). If the
peoples fail to price their dispossession in "justifiable" terms or do
not agree to the conditions set before them, they risk losing state
care, compensation and recognition altogether.

To a growing extent, states are not the only operators on the lands that
indigenous peoples have traditionally occupied. The neoliberal drive for
privatisation has taken over, and states also have their role in pushing
such development. As Deirdre Howard-Wagner, Maria Bargh and Isabel
Altamirano-JimÃ©nez (2018, 16) note in their book on neoliberal state
recognition and indigenous rights, "government policies actively
encourage private-public partnerships with industry, assuming indigenous
communities and industry are equal". Colin Samson (2016) has observed a
similar development towards privatisation. His nuanced analysis focuses
on Comprehensive Land Claims processes in Canada and their ramifications
for indigenous communities and their economic position. He notes how,
instead of the communities themselves being beneficiaries of those
processes, it is largely the resource extraction companies and
non-indigenous workers who ultimately benefit. Samson's research urges
us to view the land claims processes critically in the light of
neoliberal governance. He specifies the nature of the processes as
follows:

[By presenting extractive industries as providers of money and jobs to
Aboriginal populations, attention is easily deflected from the
disproportionate gains they make from the transfer of collective to fee
simple ownership.]{.underline} **[Less attention is also devoted to the
adulteration of the natural ecologies that indigenous histories,
communities and economies have been built upon, signalling that it is
not only land dispossession, but cultural dispossession]{.underline}**.
(Samson 2016, 99)

**[Indeed, there is a paradoxical relation between rights and neoliberal
marketisation. The state-granted rights, namely, land rights, which are
assumed to provide security for the peoples, their cultures and lands,
have led to a situation that actually opens up these lands and cultures
to business interests.]{.underline}** Without question, indigenous
communities should have equal opportunities to benefit from the
resources. However, the starting points for "doing business" are not
necessarily favourable to indigenous peoples. As Howard-Wagner, Bargh
and Altamirano-JimÃ©nez (2018, 16) point out, neoliberal privatisation
does not take into consideration the significance of past and present
"processes of land dispossession".

In a similar structural critique, Elizabeth Gail Russell (2015) points
out how, once "settled" in the eyes of the states, rights and contracts
impact future possibilities. If indigenous peoples express
dissatisfaction with developments that occur after a contract has been
made, any debate can only take place within the limits and possibilities
of the contract itself. Indigenous--state relations become encapsulated
in the contract and issues beyond or around it remain off-limits.
Russell also sees contract-making between indigenous peoples and states
as highly neoliberal in focus, that is, as serving state and corporate
interests.

The question of dispossession brings forth the connection between
neoliberalism and colonialism. **[Cameron's (2015) work, in particular,
highlights the role that land claim agreements play in sustaining
colonial relations]{.underline}**. [Her critique targets the structures
created following these agreements, asserting that they enable colonial
and neoliberal governance. Summarising her argument, she states that the
institutions established through land claim agreements often reinforce
and perpetuate colonial relations. **They also, as is clear in Nunavut
today, facilitate and accelerate the accumulation of capital by
formalizing title to Indigenous lands and implementing property and
governance regimes that enable resource extraction.**]{.underline}
(Cameron 2015, 18--19)

Ultimately, what calls for critique is the often unseen connection
between rights, neoliberalism and colonialism. As noted in Chapter 4 on
contractuality, rights do not always bring justice. The same logic
applies in the case of rights pertaining to resources and economic
profit. Rights do, however, engender hopes. In the context of indigenous
peoples and land rights, those hopes centre on economic security,
employment and wealth. One of the key hopes of the peoples is regaining
control of their lands. How and whether these hopes eventually
materialise is another question as the critical discussion also
demonstrates.

Ghassan Hage (2003; 2009), studying the functions of hope in capitalist
societies, points out the uneasy position of hope in neoliberalism. His
book Against Paranoid Nationalism. Searching for Hope in a Shrinking
Society (2003) delves into the profound inequality that exists in
capitalist societies and into the ways in which those societies
distribute hope. When taken to the extreme, as Hage (2003, 17) argues,
"certain groups are not offered any hope at all". The capitalist and
neoliberal state that rations hope provides it unequally and leaves some
without it altogether, produces uncertainty, dissatisfaction and
instability. In Hage's words (2003, 20), "\[a\]s the state withdraws
from society and the existing configuration of hope begins shrinking,
many people, \[...\] have begun suffering various forms of hope
scarcity". This loss of societal hope leads to "paranoid nationalism" --
a state where "no-hopers" experience "a sense of entrapment, of having
nowhere to go" (Hage 2003, 20, 21). Hage also makes an insightful
observation on how, due to history, hope or hopefulness have a different
relevance for different groups of peoples. He specifically recognises
the difference between indigenous peoples and their historically limited
hopes and the newly emerged scarcity of hope affecting other groups. As
he puts it:

They \[the newly marginalised\] join the already overmarginalised
populations of indigenous communities, homeless people, poor immigrant
workers, and the chronically unemployed. But like these groups, the
newly marginalised are not used to their state of marginality. They are
not used to being denied a share of hope by society. So they don't know
how to dig for new forms of hope. (Hage 2003, 20--21)

**[In Hage's view, the political distribution of hope is tied to the
neoliberal logic of cost-effectiveness]{.underline}**. For the
neoliberal state, it might not be "profitable" to give out hope
endlessly or in equal shares -- as if a certain amount of hope scarcity
would encourage individuals to be more active and entrepreneurial.

The distribution of hope is strongly intertwined with the promises of
economic rewards. In her study on the role of hope for the Zapatista
struggle in Mexico, Lia Haro (2010) discusses the prevailing political
economic logic of "if, then". She summarises it as follows:

If you allow us free economic reign and ensure ideal business
conditions, then you can hope for improvement and overcome social ills
enough to take advantages of enormous opportunities that we offer to
you. [As many communities in the Global South have learned, the
achievement of that dream requires the pawning of human lives, land and
resources to corporations that 'invest' in development while extracting
significant profit of their own and maintaining the social relations
intact that serve the freedom of capital \[...\] Here, the 'poor' and
'minorities' may hope, within the terms and practices prescribed, for
the 'better' world defined by institutions \[...\]. (Haro 2010,
191)]{.underline}

Haro continues by noting that this form of "hegemonic hope" does not
disturb the wealth and political position of the elite. In other words,
neoliberally engineered hope works for the benefit of the prevailing
political and economic order. At the same time, by signalling hope for
wealth and improvements to those who have less, it furthers their
dispossession

**[Contemporary indigenous--state relations can thus be described as a
meshing of colonial mentalities, neoliberal rationalities and politics
of hope]{.underline}**. The impact of neoliberal parameters becomes
visible in the ways in which resource debates, privatisation and
cost-effectiveness define contract-making between indigenous peoples and
states. It is assumed that the peoples can enter into these agreements
on an equal footing with states and the business sector, yet this
assumption reflects the persistence of colonial structures and the
historical amnesia regarding the significance that the past has for the
present. At the same time, the right of indigenous peoples to become
part of market-driven resource politics is presented as a source of hope
(see also Guerrieri 2019). However, yet again, hopes in this regard are
directed towards the future and, as the critical scholarship
demonstrates, realising these hopes can be extremely complicated. Hence,
the seeming abundance of hope entailed in neoliberal relations between
indigenous peoples and states actually produces a scarcity of hope as it
limits the peoples' room for manoeuvre. For those indigenous peoples who
are in dire need of economic support, the cash relief promised by the
business sector is difficult to pass. This is further accentuated in
situations where states have devolved some of their authority to the
peoples and, accordingly, withdrawn from some of their earlier
responsibilities to provide services and support. The state-induced
expectation is that the peoples should manage their own affairs in this
neoliberal equation. Here one would do well to ask, which hopes and
whose hopes does this setting serve?

### Link - Soft left -- State care of indigenous people

#### Murderous care link -- the affirmative's attempt to reconciliate colonial issues between indigenous people and the state results in [murderous care]{.underline} programs that place [false hope]{.underline} in violent solutions -- the Arctic proves. 

**Lindroth and Sinevaara-Niskanen**, Lindroth is a researcher of
critical studies in the Arctic at the University of Lapland --
Sinevaara-Niskanen has a PhD and is a Senior Lecturer at the University
of Lapland for Critical Arctic Studies**, 2022**

\[Marjo and Heidi, Ch. 5 Colonialism in the grammar of hope, in The
Colonial Politics of Hope: Critical Junctures of Indigenous--State
Relations, 2022, Routledge,
https://www.routledge.com/The-Colonial-Politics-of-Hope-Critical-Junctures-of-Indigenous-State-Relations/Lindroth-Sinevaara-Niskanen/p/book/9780367755676?srsltid=AfmBOor70ZCeUrnPqUC7ktaPDvqZ4AUdI9jD2gquQWJshJEt3rlcAq6Z,
pgs. 97-101, HDCSW -- Accessed July 15 2025\]

Hopeful care

As we see it, an aspect of care has entered into contemporary
indigenous--state relations. [**States have adopted a more inclusive,
embracing and concerned mentality in response to the** criticism that
they have received **for their previous policies and actions towards
indigenous peoples**. States' willingness to initiate truth and
reconciliation processes, to offer apologies and to increasingly
recognise the peoples' rights **all point to well-meaning care for
them**]{.underline}. This newly emerged will to care has been also taken
as a signal of hope. Clearly, care -- as a mode of behaviour -- stands
in stark contrast to previous state policies. As the shift to care from
what was in many cases eradication has been much desired, this
transition has received far less critical attention. We argue that
illuminating the dynamics of care in indigenous--state relations is
crucial to understanding the operation of hope in these relations.

The logic behind states' care is that indigenous peoples and their
causes matter and that they have been acknowledged. [Even if a state is
"not-yet" able to address or correct the injustices faced by the
peoples, there is promise that it will do so in the (near)
future.]{.underline} Truth and reconciliation processes have been widely
perceived as gestures of care and genuine attempts on the part of states
to atone for their colonial deeds. [Words such as "healing" and
"redress" readily appear in the context of these processes, where the
aim is to openly discuss and document the injustices suffered by the
victims of the colonial state.]{.underline} The underlying principle is
that after the process, the indigenous people in question, and the
society at large -- the state included -- may move forward and establish
a healthy relationship. The truth and reconciliation processes between
states and indigenous peoples that have been initiated and completed
amply exemplify this mentality (e.g. Kuokkanen 2020; Niezen 2017; Regan
2011).

[Canada has pioneered state-led reconciliation efforts]{.underline}.
From 2008 to 2015, the country went through a truth and reconciliation
process focused on its residential school history. While the state
invested time and financial resources in the process, significant
questions have remained as to how the knowledge and experience gathered
will affect the future relations of indigenous peoples and the rest of
the society. Offering a profound critique of the process, Ronal Niezen
(2017) questions the principles on which the work of the Truth and
Reconciliation Commission was built. In his book Truth and Indignation:
Canada's Truth and Reconciliation Commission on Indian Residential
Schools (2017), Niezen criticises, for example, the victim-centred
approach of the process and the way in which the state distanced itself
from the process. A process that was presented as a sign of care and as
a way towards improved relations between indigenous peoples and state
produced, in many ways, the opposite outcome. As Niezen (2017, 20)
points out, the mandate of the commission was quite limited from the
very outset. Only certain educational institutions were included, and in
encouraging particular kinds of narratives the process left out others.
In other words, the commission sought only particular truths and
testimonies. One consequence of the process centring on the victims is
that it left out the structural basis of harms done. In Niezen's words,

we hear a great deal about the pain, struggle, and redemption that
characterize a particular type of survivor experience, while our
knowledge of the "perpetrators," their motives, and the institutions
that harboured them are left obscured. (Niezen 2017, 168--169)

Niezen (2017, 169) continues, asserting that this focus on survivors and
the limited mandate of the commission led to its being "unable to
effectively function with reference to the actual causes of warped
disciplinarity, the sources of misguided policies that were the
immediate source of the collective harm it sought to address". Indeed,
**[as the fundamental structures of the colonial state were left
unqueried, Niezen concludes that the promise of genuine and lasting
reconciliation was not fulfilled.]{.underline}**

[Finland has also taken steps towards reconciliation with the SÃ¡mi
people, in response to an initiative put forward by the SÃ¡mi
Parliament]{.underline}. [The proposal for a truth and reconciliation
commission was approved by the SÃ¡mi Parliament and the Skolt SÃ¡mi
Village Committee in 2019 and the election of commissioners is underway
at the time of writing this book]{.underline}. The aim of the process is
to recognise and assess historical and present discrimination, including
efforts by the state to assimilate the SÃ¡mi. In addition, the commission
is tasked with looking for ways to improve the relations between the
SÃ¡mi and the state of Finland. The commission's mandate explicitly
states that as a result of the process "Finland will bear its
responsibility" and, in collaboration with the SÃ¡mi, "will strengthen
the fulfilment of the rights of the SÃ¡mi" (Saamelaisten totuus- ja
sovintokomission asettaminen 2019, 3). It is worth noting here that the
truth and reconciliation process is linked to the question of the
ratification of ILO Convention 169. Rauna Kuokkanen (2020) offers a
critical assessment of the points of departure in the process.
**[Echoing Niezen's observation, she questions the extent to which the
structural basis of contemporary SÃ¡mi--state relations allows genuine
reconciliation. Summarising the concerns of the SÃ¡mi, she
asks:]{.underline}**

**[How can we trust the state as a party to reconciliation while for the
past several years, it has been backtracking on (already limited) SÃ¡mi
rights, legislation and established state obligations toward the SÃ¡mi?
How can the state genuinely engage in reconciliation while pushing for
aggressive and extensive land use and development plans considered
detrimental to the interests of the SÃ¡mi, particularly to traditional
livelihoods?]{.underline}** (Kuokkanen 2020, 294)

**[Kuokkanen concludes that without substantial structural
transformation in the relations between the SÃ¡mi and the state, the
truth and reconciliation process has slim chances of success. To judge
from existing relations and their biased grounds, the process will only
serve to perpetuate colonialism]{.underline}** in Finland Providing an
account of the question of reconciliation in international relations at
large, Catherine Lu (2017, 200) notes how "\[s\]tructural dignity is a
fundamental prerequisite of a just social structure and an objective
component of structural reconciliation". Indeed, the reorganisation of
indigenous--state relations that purportedly takes place through
reconciliation requires a thorough and honest assessment of how and why
these relations are what they are today. That reconciliation processes
have been conducted is no evidence that they drew on such a structural
evaluation; rather, they seem to be essentially one-off processes
through which the states can convey the message of an issue being
"settled", once and for all (see also Niezen 2017). **[In this respect,
the idea of care that is embedded in reconciliation is
misleading,]{.underline}** as the processes largely betoken piecemeal
attention to issues that the states have been willing to address. Issues
that are too complex or compromise state interests have been overlooked,
despite their significance to the indigenous peoples in question.

Critical legal scholarship has also observed the structural foundations
of the exclusion of indigenous peoples. For example, Kathleen Birrell
(2016), Irene Watson (2015) and Stephen Young (2020) have revealed the
colonial basis of rights and legal institutions. In her book
Indigeneity: Before and Beyond the Law (2016), Birrell notes how there
is a certain type of indigenous subject that the law constructs and
requires as needing protection. For those recognised as indigenous and
in need of protection, as she points out, state recognition is
"violently determinative" in the moment when indigeneity is recognised
(Birrell 2016, 16). Here "violence" refers to the need law has to
categorise the indigenous as Other and to expect authenticity of it.
Ultimately then, as Birrell argues, the attempts of indigenous peoples
to gain justice through Western law are extremely difficult. Along
similar lines, Irene Watson (2015; 2017) demonstrates how in Australia
settler law has been, and still is, a vehicle through which the
colonising project has been advanced and normalised. Reflecting on the
question of claiming rights, Young (2020, 19) also notes the problems
endemic to state-created legal systems. [In his words, by taking part in
these legal frameworks an indigenous subject becomes "identifiable as a
legal subject who has, whether one intends to or not, already professed
obedience to the powers that one conscripts". Through law, the peoples
become agents and objects of oppression at the same time**. For
indigenous peoples, claiming rights within the Western legal domain is
thus a double-edged sword**]{.underline}.

**[As indicated above, if indigenous peoples are to be recognised in the
eyes of the law, the expectation is that they will represent themselves
in a recognisable manner. Only in this way can the peoples be recognised
as legal subjects and worthy of the state's care. Such care is limited,
however, as it only recognises indigenous subjects as either
authentically indigenous or as victims. Such a biased setting derives
from Western and colonial law being founded by the states and thus
favourable to their worldview and aspirations.]{.underline}** Overall,
what we see is an uneasy "fit" between indigenous peoples and their
claims and states' regimes of recognition and redress. Even though there
is a tendency to think that law is neutral in its pursuit of justice,
it, too, has its colonial heritage.

**[In Life Beside Itself: Imagining Care in the Canadian Arctic, Lisa
Stevenson (2014) adds another layer to the critical discussion on
continuing colonial practices and the allegedly well-meaning care of
states for indigenous peoples. Delving into the topic of welfare, she
traces the ways in which welfare colonialism has appeared over time in
Canada's policies towards the Inuit]{.underline}**. Her particular focus
is care and how it operates in governing development. Offering a
biopolitical analysis of the tuberculosis epidemic in the 1950s and
1960s and of the current suicide crisis in Inuit communities, Stevenson
demonstrates how the state's care is indifferent. **[Taking this
argument further, she calls the care received by the Inuit
"murderous".]{.underline}** As her nuanced analysis reveals, the Inuit
have been included in the state's anonymous and bureaucratic care, which
is only interested in keeping the Inuit alive. This care works, as
Stevenson (2014, 73) puts it "to maintain the physical life of Inuit qua
Canadian citizens**[". At the same time, it represents "murderous
indifference", because the state and its agencies "promote cooperation
in a regime of life, but they also expect that Inuit will
fail]{.underline}**" (Stevenson 2014, 73**[). In this mode of caring
"the death of the tubercular or the suicidal Inuk comes as no surprise"
(]{.underline}**Stevenson 2014, 7). **[The care, which in Stevenson's
(2014, 4) words is "couched by the state in terms of benevolence and
concern" is, in effect, an outright expectation that the Inuit will fail
to live.]{.underline}** Here one sees welfare mechanisms, whose express
purpose is to provide care, overlook indigenous peoples' needs. [All in
all, Stevenson's study demonstrates the way in which state care
continues to be destructively colonial, even in the intimate spheres of
healthcare and suicide prevention]{.underline}.

**[Indeed, care has unintended consequences, much like hope
has]{.underline}**. Miriam Ticktin (2011) also takes up the troubling
aspects of care in her book Casualties of Care: Immigration and the
Politics of Humanitarianism in France. Drawing on a broader discussion
on humanitarian immigration, she argues that certain groups of people
become "casualties of care". Her analysis focuses on asylum seekers and
the way in which they have to "earn" the care of the state through
biological suffering. As Ticktin demonstrates, the racialised
postcolonial nation state has deep roots. This becomes particularly
evident when the only way for asylum seekers to gain access to the
services of the state is suffering -- providing evidence of sickness,
disability or being victims of violence. In the state's politics of
care, such victimhood follows subjects for they become visible only as
gendered and racialised victims who can never be equal (Ticktin 2011).
While Ticktin's account does not address indigenous peoples, a similar
logic of partial recognition and colonial structures is at play.

The intentions of states to care for indigenous peoples appear
problematic, whether one looks at the issue from the perspective of
politics, law or welfare.

**[State-provided care tends to signal hope for the better, but when one
scratches the promising surface, the less heartening colonial
foundations become evident. The very care that was meant to heal and
renew indigenous--state relations is dangerous.]{.underline}** Elizabeth
Povinelli (2011) builds a similar type of argument in her work Economies
of Abandonment. Social Belonging and Endurance in Late Liberalism.
Weaving together questions of liberal recognition, a promise of care and
the conditions of Aboriginal people in Australia, she illustrates the
contemporary functioning of abandonment. Povinelli captures the core
problem in state efforts to care for the colonised when she asks, "What
does care consist of such that when a form of creating harm for others
is thought of and experienced as a form of caring for others?"
(Povinelli 2011, 58). As she then demonstrates, for indigenous peoples
in Australia, such care has only brought on calls for endurance and
resulted in the peoples being abandoned by the state. The seemingly
benevolent deeds of the state under the guise of care have meant that
the peoples must "persist in potentiality" (Povinelli 2011, 128).
Povinelli elaborates the point, saying "that they will have to live with
less now in order to live with more in the future, or that their present
deaths are actually a future redemption" (Povinelli 2011, 99). Our
perception of hope and its political valence resonates with Povinelli's
idea of waiting and "persisting in potentiality", as they share the same
elements of a future orientation and promise of a future reward The
above engagement with critical scholarship demonstrates that where
indigenous peoples and the structural foundations of colonialism are
concerned, care and hope are entwined. [Hope is not far removed from the
promises of care, and acts of care reproduce hope]{.underline}. [As we
argue, the colonial state has succeeded in harnessing this dynamic of
hopeful care for its benefit]{.underline}. In providing more or less
explicit expressions of its willingness to care, a state can be seen as
implementing a politics of hope that enables it to extend its rule over
indigenous peoples, once again. This is illustrated both in the states'
power to decide the timing and the extent of the legal, political or
social care they dispense and in their authority to not alter the
colonial structure of their institutions of care. [The cruel dynamics of
state care leads us to ponder the question, if and when care can be
murderous, can hope be lethal]{.underline}? Biopolitical scholarship, in
general, has brought to the fore the ways in which care is harnessed for
the exercise of governmental power. Indeed, part of contemporary
politics is that we are "cared to death" (Dillon 2005). **[As we see it,
hope and the politics of hope are part and parcel of this continuum of
state care.]{.underline}** In the case of indigenous peoples and their
rights, the entanglement of care and hope becomes particularly critical
as the two are located within the boundaries of colonial
intelligibility: care and hope have a particular meaning in the language
that colonial states speak. **[Consequently, the care and hope that the
peoples receive remain within the confines of the state's colonial
structures.]{.underline}**

## 1NC Alts

### Alt -- Combat Breath

#### The Alternative is a violent challenging of normative and popular understandings of the colonialized and black body through the combat breath

**Agathangelou,** Associate professor in the department of political
science at York University focused on Postcolonial Theory, International
Political Economy, Transnational Feminism, and Racialized Bodies in
world politics**. 2011**

Anna M., "Bodies to the Slaughter: Global Racial Reconstructions,
Fanon's Combat Breath, and Wrestling for LIfe", Edinburgh University
Press, 03/22/2011, www.eupjournals.com/soma, accessed: 07/10/2025
HDCSW - LA

Fanon repeatedly articulates this antagonistic relationship as if he
wants to split asunder the dominant and normative belief that slavery
and colonialism consolidate politics to fit all desires and serve the
interests of all.

Instead, Fanon argues **[that slavery and colonialism create in the
native a tendency towards violence, a 'tonicity of muscles' deprived of
an outlet]{.underline}**. On both personal and social registers**[, men
can free themselves socio-ontologically once this violence is
expressed:]{.underline}** 'For the native, life can only spring up again
out of the rotting corpse of the settler' (Fanon 1952: 43). He adds:

The mobilization of the masses, when it arises out of the war of
liberation, introduces into each man's consciousness the ideas of a
common cause, a national destiny and of a collective history. In the
same way the second phase, that of building up the nation is helped by
the existence of this cement which has been mixed with blood and anger.
(Fanon 1952: 83)

Fanon disrupts the dominant idea that the colonised are dominated by
contingent machinations of the state and war. He argues that **[natives
and blackness are constituted by violence ontologically in the first
instance.]{.underline}** So politics for Fanon is a politics of
ontology. In my view, his philosophical gesture is not arbitrary or
necessarily metaphysical in the modern sense; thus, he insists on
talking about the slave and the colonised and the tensions that emerge
in the erection of the species and the flesh. **[When he mentions the
native's life springing up from 'the rotting corpse of the settler', he
not only incites violence by employing its grammar but also challenges
the normative and popular understandings of the materiality of violence
and our understanding the body itself]{.underline}**. In effect, in
explaining what 'combat breath' is in the everyday struggles against the
coloniser whose project is the final destruction of the colonised, he is
also able to point to the reasons why combat breathing enables
alternative worlds and worldviews.

**[There is not occupation of territory, on the one hand, and
independence of persons on the other. It is the country as a whole, its
history, its daily pulsation that are contested, disfigured, in the hope
of a final destruction. Under these conditions, the individual's
breathing is an observed, an occupied breathing. It is a combat
breathing.]{.underline}** From this point on, the real values of the
occupied quickly tend to acquire a clandestine form of existence. In the
presence of the occupier, the occupied learns to dissemble, to resort to
trickery. (Fanon 1967c: 65)

Anaximenes said**[: 'It is breathing, not simple air, that
individualizes the human being, that gives him or her subjectivity, and
that ultimately constitutes his or her soul. Such an activity of
breathing provides physiological as well as psychological, physical as
well as spiritual life;]{.underline}** and in this sense, more than a
material element (as air is), pneuma is a force, a life-force' (quoted
in Benso 2008: 14). What politics can empty this life force? If pneuma,
air, and psyche 'interact and feed each other \... \[and\] each
nourishes the other' (Anaximenes quoted in Benso 2008: 16), **[what
happens when this breathing is disrupted by violent bloody means?
Nothing less than ontological slaughtering unless one already presumes
that breathing or what makes a human being a subject is only reserved
for the coloniser, the settler who cannot hear the breathing of the
slave and the colonised]{.underline}**. Fanon recognises that **[the
'stability' of the coloniser's being is directly connected to the
native's hallucinatory whitening that is, annihilation and also his/her
ontological suffocation. Ultimately, for this antagonism to be
disrupted,]{.underline}** that is the suffocation, that theft of air
that is assumed to be required for stability, **[violence is
required:]{.underline}**

**[Decolonization which sets out to change the order of the world, is,
obviously, a program of complete disorder \... Decolonization is the
meeting of two forces, opposed to each other by their very
nature]{.underline}** which in fact owe their originality to that sort
of substantification which results from and is nourished by the
situation in the colonies \... The native and the settler are old
acquaintances. In fact, the settler is right when he speaks of knowing
'them' well. (Fanon 1967d: 36)

Fanon first distinguishes the materiality of the life force from the
fact of property relations. For him, **[without politico-ontological
antagonisms about this life force there is no coherency in property
relations and juridical subject formations. However, these
politico-ontological antagonisms cannot be transformed and consolidated
into a supposedly ordered world]{.underline}**. Foucault presumes that
war and biopolitics remain technologies of societal coherence even when
he recognises that the idea of the universal subject is constituted by
force, but Fanon disrupts this dominant idea. For him, direct force is
not merely the theft of land and labor but that mere breath that keeps
alive the ongoing-relationship of ecologies and being. **[Consequently,
for Fanon,]{.underline}** **[while decolonisation emerges out of these
antagonisms, its constitutive energy is the combat breath itself -- the
disruptive force required for transformation]{.underline}**.

#### Combat Breath: the lived struggle of the slave and colonized subject, is vital in disrupting the spatial schism of ecology and body, in a violent state that exerts direct force and extermination for the sake of securing world order. 

**Agathangelou, Associate professor in the department of political
science at York University focused on Postcolonial Theory, International
Political Economy, Transnational Feminism, and Racialized Bodies in
world politics. 2011**

Anna M., "Bodies to the Slaughter: Global Racial Reconstructions,
Fanon's Combat Breath, and Wrestling for Life", Edinburgh University
Press, 03/22/2011, www.eupjournals.com/soma, pgs. 209-211, accessed:
07/10/2025 HDCSW -- LA

'Past Over Present', about the African struggles published in Al-Ahram,
an online Cairo newspaper, engages present tensions in the possibilities
for political transformation (Nkrumah 2000). Gamal Nkrumah, son of Kwame
Nkrumah, a founder of Pan-Africanism, opens with a photo of seven
chained slaves; the accompanying text states that in German South-West
Africa, the Herero uprising against European settler colonialism was
ruthlessly put down when the Germans killed 500,000 people. **[Nkrumah
argues that today's assembling of this European-world-order of violence
and instability depends on negotiation and peace but also on the
extermination of millions in the name of democracy. He comments on the
critical need to]{.underline}** do what Fanon talks about in The
Wretched of the Earth (1967d**[): disrupt and transform the central
relation of ontological violence that it takes to re-assemble and
reconstruct imperial powers]{.underline}**.1 Foreseeing the crises
generated by the postwar liberal international order, African, Arab, and
Asian leaders designed and worked to implement an alternative to this
order, or what Siba Grovogui (2007) calls 'multiple orders of
justification' which were interested in addressing 'diverse political
and moral concerns', seeking to put in place the contours of a global
'order of justification' that embodied justice, freedom, and democracy.
Their interventions disrupted the dominant methods of reassembling
imperial-sovereignties, opening up space for not only Africans but for
all colonised peoples to avoid naturalising and essentialising their
ongoing struggles; now they could 'reread them and reposition \... them
in terms of the new problem-space, the new context of questions, that
define\[d\] their present' (Scott 1999: 224).

These multiple struggles point to the problem-spaces of 'embodiment'
(Bosnak 2007; Ihde 2001; Seremetakis 1994; MerleauPonty 1962) and
Fanon's theory of 'combat breath' (Fanon 1967c: 65**[). Fanon
understands 'combat breathing' as the lived struggle of the slave and
colonised subject contending with foreign violence and surveillance and
the 'monopolizing notion of humanity that gives to the colonizer's
values all ontological weight']{.underline}** (Maldonado-Torres 2005:
154). **[He sees freedom as a structuring ontology, not just a political
experience and practice;]{.underline}** this gestures back to the
Stoics, for whom air, breath and soul are one. Anaximenes states that
'our soul (yuch), being air (aera&), holds us together, so do breath
(pneuBma) and air (aera&) encompass the whole world. Yuch is air; air
and pneuma are synonyms; therefore the soul is also pneuma' (Anaximenes
1898, quoted in Benso 2008: 14; Vamvacas 2009). I read Fanon's idea of
combat breath to point beyond violence to the relation of the
materiality and immateriality of the breath (pneuBma) to open up a
'different idea of the polis from that envisioned by the metaphysical
(and Roman) West' (Spanos 2000: 206). I work with Fanon to understand
the new problem-space that shapes our present. Combat breath, I argue,
is not an event of the past, or what remains after slavery and
colonialism, nor the 'end' of anti-colonial projects (such as
development, national liberation, socialisms and communisms) but living
practices constituted in involuntary constrictions of specific peoples'
lives. **[With Fanon, I articulate combat breath as lived existential
struggles that bring together the 'infinite and the finite without
collapsing them into a
monolithicprocess']{.underline}**(ZËizËekandMilibank2009:17). **[This
combat breath of the living disrupts and exceeds the dominant implicit
spatial schism of ecology and body, sovereign subject and its soma or
flesh, person and living being]{.underline}** (the phenomenon and the
noumenon) **[in political projects and their desired contingent
orders.]{.underline}**

**[Since the 1990s, political projects changing the world order and
institutions alike have involved intensified war and
slaughter]{.underline}** (Goodman 2010), **[pauperisation, and
restructuring institutions such as the state and the market, ecologies,
and corporealities in the name of progress]{.underline}**. Such
restructuring has become the focus of contestation in many parts of the
world, though often in more complex ways than most analyses acknowledge:
what kinds of expenditures, for instance, are required to erect such
order(s) and sovereign bodies, and make viable such projects?

Worldwide, a diverse (neo) liberal leadership has intensified its
involvement in (post) and (neo) reconstructions by focusing on grammars
and ethics of suffering in the name of security and humanitarian
regimes**[. Direct force is extended and refined in the name of 'saving
the state and the market' and securing world peace and
order]{.underline}**; venturist states and markets stalk opportunity by
leveraging somata's 'surpluses' (taxes, extraction of more value from
labour, sale of body parts and 'body bits', regeneration of bodies),
**[buffering the state's capacity to incur substantial debts and carry
out executions, whether this entails deployment of forces, structural
financial repairs, policing action, or suffocating
people.]{.underline}** Such interventions point to the deployment and
limits of temporal and spatial reassemblages and derivative
reconstructions across political, social, economic, and corporeal
registers. Thematically speaking, **[derivative reconstructions disclose
the power of a derivative-slave-colonisation death discourse with
definite practices and purposes: the use of vital energies of 'black'
states, slaves, and 'black' ecologies even at the moment of their
execution to generate capable somata (such as land,
bodies)]{.underline}**.2

innocence**, stands in contrast to aims of reconciliation, which
motivate settler moves to innocence. Reconciliation is about rescuing

decolonization look like? What** will happen **after** abolition**?**
**What** will be* *the **consequences** of decolonization **for the

Oakland and other settlers who want to know what decolonization will
require of them. [The answers are not fully in view and can't be as long
as decolonization remains punctuated by metaphor. The **answers** will
not emerge from friendly understanding, and indeed **require** a
**dangerous understanding** of uncommonality that un-coalesces coalition
politics - moves that may feel very unfriendly. But we will find out the
answers as we get there, "in the exact measure that we can discern the
movements which give \[decolonization\] historical form and
content"]{.underline} (Fanon, 1963, p. 36). To fully enact an ethic of
incommensurability means relinquishing settler futurity, [**abandoning**
the **hope** that **settlers may** one day **be commensurable to
Native** peoples. It means removing the asterisks, periods, commas,
apostrophes, the whereas's, buts, and conditional clauses that punctuate
decolonization and underwrite settler innocence. The Native futures, the
lives to be lived once the settler nation is gone - these are the
**unwritten possibilities** made possible by an ethic of
incommensurability.]{.underline}

### Alt -- Epistemological Rejection

#### **The alternative is [fundamentally dismantling]{.underline} the ["sexual affective empire"]{.underline} seeped in [colonial]{.underline}, [racialized]{.underline}, [affective-epistemological]{.underline} structures that dictate who matter by [building a global politics]{.underline} that [rejects]{.underline} the [violent hierarchies]{.underline}. Embracing [plural]{.underline}, inclusive, [models]{.underline} of [being]{.underline}, [knowing]{.underline}, and [relating]{.underline}.**

**Agathangelou 2019** (Professor Anna M. Agathangelou is a political
scientist and IR theorist from York University in Toronto. She is the
co-director of Global Change Institute, Cyprus. She has researched
ethnic conflict in Cyprus, as well as the challenges of missing persons
and DNA identification of remains in post-conflict societies.), "Ch. 12
-- Sexual Affective Empires", *Methodology and Emotion in International
