Sucharov), 2019, pgs. 210-212

What IR wagers say about passion and unthinkable feelings and global
politics In the introduction to this volume, the editors defined
**[wagers as "the choice]{.underline}** is **[made prior to the conduct
of empirical research]{.underline}** which subsequently structure
research design." This definition **[is important, especially for
conventional IR,]{.underline}** which has given little thought to the
politics of the choices made before conducting empirical research period
rather, much of such literature assumes that empirics are not informed
by assumptions and wages made about the world. To pose the question
about the choices made prior to conducting empirical research demands A
literacy that cannot be reduced to script lively to what we already
know. It forces us to think, yet is by definition beyond the capture of
what things say. Normally, this demands to think binds thought to
practical principles and reasons, but it paradoxically loses its hold,
and we are confronted with what different modes of thinking trust in,
that is, with the assumptions they make and the risks they take. The
merriam Webster dictionary defines wager as: a colon something (such as
a sum of money) risked on an uncertain event: steak B: something on
which bets are laid: gamble do a stunt as a wager and its archaic
definition: and act of giving a pledge to take and abide by the result
some action. Interestingly, [this definition speaks to the stakes of
taking risks and gambling]{.underline} (as if they are segregated
activities) and to the act of gambling itself, both of which point to
speculative aspects, strategies as cognitive and as financial acts that
engineer future commodified by derivatives, quantified by risk
management models, and preempted in fiscal policy and military strategy,
a feature both risky and foreseeable. The making of this possibility
does not depend on utopian promises but rather on making value through a
"preponderance for risk taking with high levels of testosterone and
therefore masculinity" (Nelson 2013, cited in McClean 2016: 428). Making
these choices explicit is pivotal for undertaking any research project.
However**[, this waging approach does not challenge the onto
epistemological assumptions that inform them.]{.underline}** 211 **[this
intervention speaks more to the criteria guiding the research design. It
does not raise questions about and whether research and knowledge
production and IR as a discipline, field, and ethos are themselves
problematic in the sense that they contribute to the making of a
sex-affective-empire Co constituted with a certain liberal
order.]{.underline}** Within this assemblage those the sovereign does
attend or even pay attention to when they hear are rendered illegible as
a given social logic, or logos. **[This makes it hard to understand
imperial subjecthood for those enslaved and colonial subjects as a
meaningful subjectivity]{.underline}**. Their loss would occasion a
disorientation, a feeling of being outside of the logos. If, as i
articulated above, [affects are sorted through the axes of power of sex
and raciality that accrue their meaning enforced through assembled
colonial, enslaving, and settler colonial relations, what sorts of
methodological insights into their wagers might the researchers of the
affective turn provide to us, so that we may think their work beyond the
pragmatic choices made prior to empirical research about sex and race as
all-encompassing, dynamic, and self organizing forces?]{.underline} I
argue that **[focusing mostly on the wages,]{.underline}** [that is, the
choices and propositions researchers make with regards to research
design, **may be useful**]{.underline}. Such an approach, the wagering
on the possibility, however implausible or unlikely from the point of
view of our contemporary knowledge, and attending to the our methods and
their launching into the developing process of the world, might find a
response so that such methods themselves might become practically
configurative of the responses to the problems that the present poses.
**[This quite innovative approach could challenge not only the dominant
propositions that inscribe IR, but also the materiality of our
contemporary imperial affective assemblages]{.underline}**. **[However,
if this wagering is confined to the methodological choices, then this
strategy can only be extended to an epistemic critique. If the aim is to
challenge the assemblage of sexual affective empire thus simultaneously
contesting colonialism, settler colonialism and enslavement and the
myriad effects of inattention, neglect, and abandonment, that challenge
will not be achieved by epistemic critique, but rather by contesting the
empire in a more general description of its language of
time]{.underline}** period **[the interminable process of epistemic
critique and interpretation of phenomena and its issues of final truths
and objective facts cannot simply be adjudicated through our research
and the choices of our methods]{.underline}**. Perhaps what is at stake
here in this fine distinction and most radically the matter with IR is
not that there may be no way out of the epistemological presuppositions
through method, but more aporectically, that the binary sorting of those
whose feelings are valued and those who are not, those whose sex and
racialized identities are given attention and care and those who do not,
be become meaningless, and the ideological affects the ground impossible
as I R thought and practice. **[Pointing to this question of production
and valuation of affects as a violence requires investigating the
foundations of theories and their affective-epistemological strucutres
to understand the guidance of ontological and political practices they
propose or make possible. This demands A rethinking of dominant
epistemological frameworks within which questions of power and global
politics, violence and freedom, and knowledge are
approached.]{.underline}** 212 For this reason, wagers and assumptions
in the form of choices made before empirical research and wagers to
extend the life of our theorizations cannot be understood outside an
empire of sexual affects. **[The emergence and repurposing of sexual
affective empire solicits the production of particular ways of feeling,
being, desiring, and knowing (while simultaneously destroying others)
that make viable A reconstituted imperial world order. Central to
this]{.underline}**, as I said above, **[are affective economies,
characterized as the circulation and mobilization of feelings of desire,
fear, and pleasure that seduce all of us into becoming emotionally,
libido annually, and erotically invested in global capitalism and
imperialisms mirages of care, security, and inclusion]{.underline}**.
**[This emerging empire becomes Co constituted through "the process of
seduction \[for some\] so violence that proceeds through false promises
of an end to oppression and pain]{.underline}**" (Agathangelou,
Bassichis and Spira 2008: 122). [And more recently, to an intensified
form of speculation that does not promise any utopian
possibility.]{.underline} These affective economies are entangled with
global ruling epistemologies that enable the production of surplus value
and material relations (Agathangelou 2004: 6-12). This understanding of
structural power recognizes that current systems depend on certain forms
of violence that allow feelings and freedom for some and anxiety,
terror, and unfreedom for others. The latter need to be changed,
integrated, or killed to make possible and repurpose the
sex-affective-empire. [Researchers thus have to speak more to the onto
epistemologies that sort and organize different affects, including the
ways these onto epistemologies do not grapple with how neglect,
abandonment, or indifference are entangled with certain forms of
dominant capital and white supremacy power. **Wagers, thus, are not so
much about providing us with methodological criteria to trace a history
of passion politics, as they are a theoretical analysis of the
possibility of structure of time (IE, the future) and such politics and
the attempts to organize, order, segregate, and violently expunge some
lives and their affects and not others. They are not neutral
technologies of knowledge making and governance either.**]{.underline}
In the introduction to this volume, the editors speak of wagers and IR
and passion politics citing Jackson and Nexon, "wagers," they tell us,
"precede empirical studies and, in turn, 'cash out' in the form of
different accounts of political phenomena...\[There are\] Wagers about
epistemology\--how things and processes are knowable." While I agree, I
suggest passion politics has mobile attachments, including contingent
wager regimes that extend the life of certain frames, including certain
systems of thought and affect and not others. **[What is at stake in
feeling for a future that may be more than just an extension of the
modern sexual affective empire]{.underline}**?

## 2NC

### Role of the Judge

#### The role of the ballot is to engage with decolonial methods that does not reproduce western epistemology of Indigenous culture and voting for what best engages with purely Indigenous forms of education

**Wilson et al, Department of Geography, 19**

\[Wilson, Bell, Arreak, et al, Department of geography, Nunavut
operations lead, School of Geography and Earth Sciences, Changing the
Role of Non-Indigenous research partners in practice to support Inuit
self-determination in research, 9-9-19, NRC research press, 7-12-25,
HDCSW-S. Asse\]

The review of the literature started with the goal of understanding if
there was support and advice for non-Indigenous researchers as
methodological guidance in advance of the research. [Some Indigenous
scholars recognize that particular non-Indigenous researchers have a
"genuine desire to support the cause"]{.underline} (Smith 2012a, p. 186)
and Kovach (2009, p. 11) [believes that there is a new generation
"seeking ways to understand the world without harming it".]{.underline}
Smith (2012a) and Louis (2007) discuss that [excluding non-Indigenous
researchers would only perpetuate Indigenous research as some sort of
cultural privilege, when it\'s an opportunity for non-Indigenous
researchers to develop "the tools they need to ensure that their
research agendas are]{.underline} 'sympathetic, respectful, and [ethical
from an Indigenous perspective']{.underline}" (Louis 2007, p. 134). [To
begin "decolonizing one's mind and heart" (Kovach 2009, p. 169),
non-Indigenous researchers need to begin the "self-education process"
well in advance of the research]{.underline} (Gaudry 2015, p. 259). The
five sections that follow provide a summary from the literature that I
utilized as initial guidance and advice to develop a process and a
methodology in decolonizing myself. In this paper, [the term Indigenous
will refer to Indigenous research collectively]{.underline}. The term
Inuit will be used when specifically discussing research in Inuit
Nunangat, "the distinct geographic, political, and cultural region that
includes the Inuvialuit Settlement Region (Northwest Territories),
Nunavut, Nunavik (Northern Quebec), and Nunatsiavut (Northern Labrador)"
(ITK 2018b, p. 18). The term non-Indigenous will refer to research
partners coming from outside of Indigenous cultures.

**[As a non-Indigenous researcher I]{.underline}** needed to [**educate
myself further about the colonial history of Indigenous peoples and the
resulting and continuing trauma.**]{.underline} For the North American
Inuit, the influence of colonialism started in the 17th century, when
whalers, explorers, missionaries and Hudson's Bay Company fur traders
first came to the Canadian Arctic (ITK 2006). However, it was during the
Cold War era of the 1950s when Inuit were forced to settle in
communities as part of the Government of Canada's assimilation approach
called the "in-gathering policy" (MacDonald 2018), and some communities
were relocated into the High Arctic to further Canadian Arctic
sovereignty (CBC 2010; Qikiqtani Inuit Association 2014). As part of the
Canadian government settlement and assimilation process, Inuit children
were required to attend school and sent away to residential schools. For
an Inuit-specific understanding on the impact of colonialism in Canada,
the "Final Report of the Truth and Reconciliation Commission of Canada
Commission report Volume 2: Canada's Residential Schools: The Inuit and
Northern Experience" (TRC 2015), and Chapter 4: Colonization as Gendered
Oppression and specifically the sub-section entitled "Colonial
Encounter: Distinctive Inuit Experiences" (MMIWG 2019) are excellent
resources. T[he term post-colonial is often used to describe the current
state of affairs, but many Indigenous scholars argue that this infers
that "colonialism no longer exists"]{.underline} (Smith 2012a, p. 25).
[Colonialism in the Canadian context is described as
set]{.underline}tler [col]{.underline}onialism, in [which people from
other countries invaded, settled and established sovereign
power]{.underline} (Barker and Battell Lowman 2016).
[Se]{.underline}ttler [col]{.underline}onialism [is an ongoing process
that continues to structure and shape relations between Indigenous
peoples and settlers]{.underline} (Wolfe 2006; Tuck and Yang 2012;
Veracini 2013). Castleden et al. (2012) and Simpson (2004) both argue
that ex[ternal colonial control through ongoing federal government
policies and practices continues to marginalize Indigenous peoples in
Canada.]{.underline} Price (2007) and McGrath (2018) argue that
[colonialism continues to systematically and symbolically undermine and
devalue the Inuit cultural systems that once made them
self-sufficient.]{.underline}

### AT: Reform -- Hope DA

#### Cruel Hope DA -- faith in state action to improve indigenous lives produces [tolerance]{.underline} to colonial violence and [indefinitely]{.underline} pauses progress. 

**Lindroth and Sinevaara-Niskanen**, Lindroth is a researcher of
critical studies in the Arctic at the University of Lapland --
Sinevaara-Niskanen has a PhD and is a Senior Lecturer at the University
of Lapland for Critical Arctic Studies**, 2022**

\[Marjo and Heidi, Ch. 6 Conclusion, in The Colonial Politics of Hope:
Critical Junctures of Indigenous--State Relations, 2022, Routledge,
https://www.routledge.com/The-Colonial-Politics-of-Hope-Critical-Junctures-of-Indigenous-State-Relations/Lindroth-Sinevaara-Niskanen/p/book/9780367755676?srsltid=AfmBOor70ZCeUrnPqUC7ktaPDvqZ4AUdI9jD2gquQWJshJEt3rlcAq6Z,
pgs. 121-124, HDCSW -- Accessed July 15 2025\]

Dangers of Hope

**[At the core of the relations between indigenous peoples and states
today is the expectation that the peoples are to trust that the
envisioned improvements will materialise]{.underline}**. [However,
critical indigenous and postcolonial scholarship has contributed
extensive analyses revealing the range of problems entailed in trusting
state-led processes that promise change and improvements (e.g.
Betasamosake Simpson 2017; Niezen 2017; Povinelli 2011; Regan
2011).]{.underline} That scholarship emphasises, as we do in this book,
that colonialism has not ceased and that it continues to have a bearing
on indigenous--state relations. **[The historical slate cannot be wiped
clean, and state policies of today cannot be rid of the baggage of past
betrayals where the hopes of indigenous peoples were
concerned]{.underline}**. Because the peoples have concretely
experienced a chain of broken promises, the willingness of states to
offer hope for reconciliation can engender contradictory reactions. In a
case in point, [Rauna Kuokkanen (2020) summarises the mixed perceptions
of the SÃ¡mi regarding the recent intentions of the Finnish state to
initiate a truth and reconciliations process]{.underline}. She ponders
the unclear motives of the state, asking "why the Government of Finland
has agreed to embark on a truth and reconciliation process, given its
sketchy track record of acting upon the SÃ¡mi Parliament's requests"
(Kuokkanen 2020, 297). Tellingly, the hopes that states proffer to
indigenous peoples are often met with scepticism. Indigenous peoples
have observed how "partnership" with states is elusive: in some matters
the relationship holds, while in others states fail to honour the norm
of reciprocity that partnership implies. [The processes of recognition
in Australia, Finland and Greenland, each exemplify a dynamic in which
the genuineness of partnership may well be called into
question.]{.underline}

Despite reservations and criticism on the part of indigenous peoples,
they are in many ways compelled to negotiate with states, as the
peoples' rights and material circumstances depend on them. [The
improvements promised by states are particularly attractive to
indigenous peoples who struggle to regain what in fact are
unrelinquished rights.]{.underline} [As Betasamosake Simpson (2017)
points out,]{.underline} **[even the faintest signals of change in state
practices can encourage indigenous peoples' hopes for better-structured
and more just relations.]{.underline}** Because such change is so
intensely sought after and hoped for, even minimal gestures can be
enough to maintain a belief in it. Borrowing Betasamosake Simpson's
wording (2017, 45), being hopeful and seeing signals of change are
"attractive" for indigenous peoples. Crucially, as the developments in
our selected countries also highlight, states are well aware of this
inclination to remain hopeful and exploit this tendency for their own,
less sincere ends. This is precisely what we have termed the politics of
hope.

In previous research, hope has been understood as crucial for indigenous
peoples in initiating positive change (e.g. Dinerstein 2015; Haro 2010;
Lear 2008). However, the very same promise of transformation and
empowerment that drives the efforts of those that would go against the
dominant regime can become unfavourable to their cause. Faced with
increased calls for rights, recognition and justice by the world's
indigenous peoples, politics has drawn on the soothing, encouraging and
faith-engendering nature of hope, and continues to do so. **[Where hope
may enable indigenous peoples to resist, mobilise and drive radical
change, the states may equally operationalise hope in order to govern
the peoples]{.underline}**. This equivocality characterises politics of
hope and, as we have illustrated, politics has seized on this
ambivalence to create (novel) mechanisms of governing. Instead of
prioritising an affirmative reading of hope, one should recognise its
dual political valence positive in enabling and negative in restricting.
In respect to indigenous-- state relations, the pressing question is,
what kind of politics are states exercising in and through hope?

**[The general ethos of hopefulness enables states to act
evasively.]{.underline}** **[We see procrastination as a repeating
pattern in present-day relations between indigenous peoples and states.
Instilling hopes for rights and recognition, as the cases discussed
show, provides states with wiggle room, even in the midst of heated
debates.]{.underline}** If there is hope, there is something to look
forward to, to anticipate (something better). However, the future
trajectory of hope in any given case has profound political
significance, for it enables politics to postpone changes today. **[Hope
strips the urgency from the matters at hand as its rewards are always
yet to come.]{.underline}** **[Indeed, when transformation requires
constant anticipation, the result can very well be a state of stagnation
where the envisioned change remains elusive, never materialising,
perhaps indefinitely. This dynamic hides beneath it the ways in which
changes regarding indigenous peoples' rights are postponed and
evaded.]{.underline}**

Indigenous peoples and their struggles for rights are a case in point of
politics being able to draw on the potentially inexhaustible resource of
hope. [States insinuate hope in what they say -- "maybe" in one form or
another -- and in this way succeed in capturing the attention of those
parts of the population who are in need of hope. For states, it has been
smart to say "maybe" in certain processes of rights and recognition as
this has enabled them to avoid the worst of national and international
shame at their failures to date. **By continuously offering "maybe",
states can rely on the peoples' persistent hope, all the while foisting
the responsibility for any envisioned development upon the
peoples**]{.underline}. By no means is the exercise of such a politics
unique to indigenous issues; many other minorities globally are still in
the process of waiting for equal rights. This is the condition that Hage
(2009) calls "stuckedness".

[Indigenous peoples are well aware that states have played for time and
continue to do so when it comes to issues that challenge them.
Australia, Finland and Greenland provide examples of indigenous peoples
challenging the states' procrastination.]{.underline} The call by
Australian indigenous peoples in 2017 to establish a new political body
-- known as "Voice" -- represents a particularly cogent example (The
Uluru Statement from the Heart 2017). This collective expression of will
can be considered historic. After decades of fruitless reports and
assessments, the peoples reached out for a solution on their own terms.
The call for Voice represents a refusal to settle for the state-offered
process of recognition. Not surprisingly, the response by the Australian
state has been to commission further assessments on the recognition
process as a whole because the indigenous peoples' initiative has not
been considered politically viable (Response to Referendum Council's
Report on Constitutional Recognition 2017). It is noteworthy that the
state has refused to revise the "terms of reference" of the process to
accommodate the claims of the indigenous peoples; it has kept the
process alive, but on its own terms.

The tension in indigenous--state relations in Greenland was also
palpable in the reactions to a statement by Prime Minister Aleqa
Hammond's in 2013. In her speech at the Arctic Circle Assembly, Hammond
concluded that Greenland would become independent "within my lifetime"
(Thisted 2020, 4). The confidence and concreteness of this statement
took Denmark by surprise. Although the Act on Greenland Self-Government
explicitly states that Greenland may become independent, the directly
expressed intention to do so resulted in a heated and emotional
discussion in Denmark. Reflecting on these events, Kirsten Thisted
(2020) describes the affective dynamics involved in the relations
between indigenous peoples and their colonisers. She highlights how,
paradoxically, where an alleged partnership prevails, wanting to break
away from this -- through independence -- is seen as betrayal. Greenland
was even seen as wanting to leave a "marriage" (Thisted 2020, 8). One
could infer from this that, even though it has granted Greenland the
right to become independent, Denmark has not considered this a realistic
possibility. As there are major prerequisites for Greenland's
independence, the assumption has been that, if not outright impossible,
it will be an extremely difficult and protracted process. In this light,
it is no wonder that Hammond's reference to her "lifetime" caused upset
in Denmark, because it suddenly gave Greenlandic independence a
timeframe.

**[Indeed, indigenous peoples do engage in politics that they themselves
define and through that put pressure on states.]{.underline}** In the
political equation of indigenous-- state relations, states, however,
continue to hold the authority to determine which demands can be pursued
and the time horizon within which they might be met. Previous critical
scholarship on the development of the relations between indigenous
peoples and states has also pointed out how states' calls for waiting
and the promise of progress circumscribe the position of indigenous
peoples (e.g. Povinelli 2011; Sutton 2009). [Clearly, states have
adopted procrastination as their mode of engagement with indigenous
peoples. The prevailing ethos of promise produces a condition where
indigenous peoples must tolerate the relationships they have with the
colonisers until the peoples' demands have been resolved.]{.underline}
The ultimate challenge here is that the realisation of envisioned
improvements repeatedly slips from the present into the future. **[The
time frame for the restructured relationship is always "not-yet" and, in
the meanwhile, the status quo is maintained.]{.underline}**

### Framework -- Fiat

#### Fiat -- the act of imagination -- is necessary to reduce existential risk. 

**Beard and Hobson,** Beard is Academic Programme Manager at the Centre
for the Study of Existential Risk, University of Cambridge \-- Hobson is
a Postdoctoral Research Associate at the Centre for the Study of
Existential Risk, University of Cambridge,**2024**

\[SJ and Tom - An Anthology of Global Risk edited by SJ Beard and Tom
Hobson, Part IV -- Policy, Institutions and Impacts, September 2024,
https://www.openbookpublishers.com/books/10.11647/obp.0360, pgs 514-515,
Accessed July 14 2025 \-- HDCSW\]

Still, **[imagining what might be possible is central to any work that
talks about the conditions of the future --- whether its ostensible
concern is the mitigation of risk, or the fostering of a
utopia]{.underline}**. The contributions in this section are perhaps
conspicuous in this sense, primarily for the directness with which they
explore these political, ontological, and normative relationships
between our present and our possible futures. [In asking how a
researcher or policy-maker might contribute towards the attainment of a
safer or more survivable future through the development of policy or
through influencing the trajectory or structure of
institutions,]{.underline} the authors each draw us towards **[important
foundational assumptions related to both how we understand the world as
it is, and how we imagine it ought to be.]{.underline}** [In this way,
whilst also providing rich accounts of how policies and institutions
might evolve in response to research and understanding of specific or
systemic catastrophic risks]{.underline}, the chapters also provide an
opportunity to reflect on some of the underlying factors that condition
work in the field --- [with a range of perspectives on political,
social, geographic and temporal relationalities presented by the
authors]{.underline}. Thus, Chapter 19, The Cartography of Global
Catastrophic Governance, charts the concentrations of different
governance efforts related to catastrophic risks areas and argues for
both greater attention and greater coordination, while Chapter 18,
Pathways to Linking Science and Policy in Global Risk, provides ideas
for researchers to identify pathways to impact for their own work.

### Framework - Scenario Planning Good

#### Scenario-planning is epistemically valuable. 

**Currie,** Senior Lecturer in the Department of Sociology, Philosophy
and Anthropology at Exeter,**2024**

\[Adrian Currie - An Anthology of Global Risk edited by SJ Beard and Tom
Hobson, Ch 5. Existential Risk, Creativity and Wel-Adapted Science,
September 2024,
https://www.openbookpublishers.com/books/10.11647/obp.0360, pgs 197-201,
Accessed July 14 2025 \-- HDCSW\]

4.5 Existential risk as a crisis discipline

I have discussed a notion of creativity suitable for examination via the
economic approach. [One way of contextualising such discussions is via a
description of an]{.underline} **[epistemic situation]{.underline}**.
[An investigation's epistemic situation is the sum of the challenges
facing knowledge-generation, and the resources available for overcoming
those challenges]{.underline}. I've thus far discussed the challenges
facing paradigm X-risk investigations. Paradigm X-risks are unique,
involve wild systems, and involve secondorder ignorance. Further, they
are in the public eye, having the potential to generate over-reactions,
a loss of faith, and mismanagement. These challenges are not
insurmountable: many of them are not unique to X-risk, and so we can
take our cue from other research areas.

**[It is useful to consider X-risk as a crisis
discipline]{.underline}**. In 1985, Michael SoulÃ© developed the latter
notion by comparing conservation biology and cancer research. Both
disciplines are geared towards a particular outcome (curing cancer,
preserving biodiversity) so membership in the crisis discipline turns on
possession of a set of scientific expertise related to achieving that
outcome. In addition to ecologists, then, conservation biology includes
veterinary specialists, experts in land management, and so on. We've
already had a hint about the wide variety of disciplines involved in
X-risk --- indeed, given our second-order ignorance, it's actually
unclear which disciplines will matter. In addition to being
multi-disciplinary, crisis disciplines are normative: X-risk is not
simply in the business of describing or explaining low-probability, high
impact events, but also in ascertaining how to minimise the occurrence
and impact of such events. A final similarity concerns the need to be
tolerant of uncertainty:

A conservation biologist may have to make decisions or recommendations
about design and management before he or she is completely comfortable
with the theoretical and empirical bases of the analysis.54

In addition to having the characteristics of a crisis discipline,
uniqueness and second-order ignorance mean that X-risk studies will
often occur in evidentially impoverished circumstances. I've analysed
similar epistemic situations occurring in "historical sciences" such as
paleontology, geology, and archaeology55 and here, the success of the
sciences is best explained by appeal to the speculative, creative nature
of their approach (Alison Wylie has made similar arguments concerning
archaeology; see Wylie (1999), Chapman and Wylie (2016)).56

I've sketched a set of investigative strategies which maximise
evidential reach in historical science. **[Story-telling and
scenario-building serve to maximise the empirical links between
hypotheses]{.underline}**. Historical reconstruction doesn't simply rely
on the relationship between contemporary remains --- traces --- and the
past, but on the connections between our hypotheses about the past.
Further, such speculation often generates testable hypotheses (Currie,
2017; Currie and Sterelny, 2017): historical scientists are highly
creative in my sense. I've characterised **[historical
scientists]{.underline}** as "methodological omnivores".57
Methodological omnivores engage in two distinctive behaviours. First,
they **[construct epistemic tools and models calibrated to local
context]{.underline}** (as opposed to using general-purpose tools),
**[enabling rich data to be generated]{.underline}**. [Second, a
pluralistic, opportunistic attitude to techniques, methods and research
approaches allows a wide range of perspectives, and different types of
evidence, to be available.]{.underline} Finally, uniqueness can be
mitigated by the use of partial analogies.58

Paradigm X-risks are not precisely in the same epistemic situation as
conservation biology or paleontology --- the tragedy of the uncommons is
one difference --- but nonetheless the similarities can give us an
inkling of the epistemic strategies which a science of X-risk should
adopt. The science should be multi-disciplinary, pluralistic, and
opportunistic. Such a science meets the criteria for creativity in the
sense I discussed in Section 2. Each of these factors involve a
community that does not pool, but rather explores solution space widely.

A science of X-risk, then, should be creative

5\. Discussion

A successful science of X-risk will be creative. But, as we've seen,
contemporary scientific incentives don't often encourage creativity.
Rather, they encourage cold searches. Hence, the properties required for
studying X-risk are not promoted in scientific communities. With this in
place, I want to (1) characterise this problem abstractly: that science
is "badly adapted" for studying X-risk and (2) continue my initial
sketch of a well-adapted science of X-risk.

5.1 Well-adapted science

I've given reason to think that the incentive structures governing
science are in a sense "maladapted" for some epistemic situations,
existential risk in particular. Where that situation calls for
creativity, conservatism is improperly encouraged. In this section, I'll
characterise the problem abstractly and discuss its relationship with
the economic approach on the one hand, and with work on the relationship
between social values and science on the other.

The notion of "well adapted" I want to develop concerns whether
scientific incentives encourage the kind of work that is appropriate
given an epistemic situation:

A scientific community is well adapted to the extent that the incentives
of that community promote the attainment of desired research outcomes,
given the epistemic situation at hand.

Let's contrast being well adapted in my sense --- which is a
relationship between a set of incentives and an epistemic situation ---
and the notion of an individual scientist or community being adapted to
a set of incentives. Work in the economic approach is often not
sensitive to this difference, and often the focus is more on the latter.
Weisberg and Muldoon's landscape models and their descendants explore
how different proportions of exploration strategies might be
differentially optimal in various landscapes; Zollman's bandit models
explore how scientists might learn to adapt to an epistemic situation.
Here, an adapted scientist (or community) is one which maximises their
returns (in terms of credit or knowledge) given some set of incentives.
To be well adapted in my sense, by contrast, requires those incentive
structures to be themselves set in order to maximise the epistemic (or
other) outputs that we desire given an epistemic situation. For
instance, given the nature of X-risk, incentives in the community should
encourage creativity. So, one sense of "adaptive" concerns how well
scientific behaviours maximise payoffs given a set of incentive
structures. Another --- mine --- concerns how incentive structures might
be organised to maximise epistemic outputs. Again, such a distinction is
likely implicit in much of the economic approach, but it is useful to
make it explicit.

[Socially-inclined philosophers of science have argued that decisions
about the pursuit worthiness of a scientific investigation or enterprise
--- what makes that research program a good one to do --- turns on more
than epistemic significance]{.underline}. **[Rather, a cost-benefit
calculus is required to balance preferences for research outcomes, the
efficiency of investigative approaches to those outcomes, as well as
budgetary and ethical constraints.]{.underline}** Philip Kitcher's
approach is perhaps the clearest example.59 For him, a science is "well
ordered" to the extent that which research we pursue is decided by
deliberation which approaches the cost-benefit calculus mentioned
above.60 So, discussion of the role of values in science often draws our
attention to how the organisation of science itself affects the
efficiency of a research program: concerns about scientific organisation
and prioritisation are taken as questions of resource distribution.
Given a range of possible questions scientists might be asking, by what
principles should they direct their efforts? In short, a well-ordered
science is one which balances (1) some suitably trained ("tutored")
preferences, against (2) the efficiency of particular research programs
in meeting those preferences, and (3) the costs (considered in terms of
finances, resources, and ethics) of those programs.61

### Perm -- Epistemology fails + Materialism good

**Temin**, Associate Professor of Political Science at UMICH and
Political Theorist, 20**24**

David Myer, "A decolonial wrong turn: Walter Mignolo's epistemic

[[https://doi.org/10.1111/1467-8675.12744]{.underline}](https://doi.org/10.1111/1467-8675.12744),

Worldly Approaches - actions that are focused on practical, material,
and social aspects of life, in contrast to philosophical concerns.

In conclusion, [I]{.underline} briefly [suggest an alternative to
Mignolo's epistemic politics, which I refer to as **worldly
anticolonialism**]{.underline}. What I call [worldly anticolonialism
addresses **epistemic challenges** to *colonial categories* such as
dominant universalisms with an eye targeted to their identifiable
historically situated political effects]{.underline}. Accordingly, [an
*analysis based on worldly anticolonialism would ask how such categories
of thought take material form*]{.underline}, [in relation to specific
terrains of anticolonial political struggle that have been shaped by
variable colonial relations of power]{.underline}. One analogous
approach can be found in recent work by Mamdani(2020),who analyzes how
continued investments in the category of the"nation"in settler and
postcolonial societies alike have themselves been generated through the
politically-mediated fashioning of specific collective constituencies.
[The political identities and imaginaries of these constituencies are
deeply bound to the colonial past not only as pure epistemology but
through constitutive political and historical processes]{.underline}.7
To illustrate some more specific benefits of worldly anticolonialism as
a point of departure, I turn to the complex relationship between
organized labor struggles and Indigenous peoples' struggles.

[Consider]{.underline} that [leadership of major trade unions throughout
North America largely continues to believe that extracting more fossil
fuels on Indigenous lands will furnish some of the few remaining good
jobs in an environment of radical austerity and upward
redistribution]{.underline} (Sanicola & Williams, 2021). [This framing
of]{.underline} worker [interests makes it far easier for fossil fuel
corporations to create a profoundly antagonized field of political
contest pitting]{.underline} "workers' interests" [(jobs)]{.underline}
[against those of "Indigenous peoples" and environmental
movements]{.underline} (keeping oil in the ground)(Walia,2015).8

The [goals of more *radical* union membership and **anticolonial
movements** *must* be to present compelling ***political--economic***
alternatives based on massive decarbonization. This means
**reorienting** non-Indigenous working people toward the ways that their
*freedom and well-being* also depend---albeit differently than
Indigenous peoples themselves---upon *securing and enhancing*
**Indigenous sovereignty**]{.underline}(Klein, 2014,pp.398â407). To be
sure, such [union leaders]{.underline} and some rank-and-file union
members certainly do [*participate* in the pervasive colonial epistemic
erasure of Indigenous peoples and the perpetuation of intensely gendered
forms of colonial violence when they seek to construct new pipeline
infrastructures to secure their livelihoods]{.underline}. I want to
suggest, however, that is [not obvious at all that critical political
theorists should primarily]{.underline} (let alone, only) [foreground
this colonial epistemic arrogance per se as the primary obstacle to an
alternative anticolonial coalition praxis]{.underline}. Indeed, [a *key
goal* of worldly anticol]{.underline}onialism [would be to *reconfigure
this framing of the political situation at hand*, by imagining more
robust and compelling affinities, if not convergences, between projects
of decolonization and decarbonization that would enhance the well-being
of people and the planet]{.underline}.9

This is so because [politics requires persuasion and organizing that can
reorient the interests and horizon making capacities of political
subjects]{.underline}. The [*obstacles* to this *are not simply
epistemic* in the sense of diagnosing and deconstructing colonial world
views]{.underline}. Instead, [they are rooted in real interests, fears,
hopes, and soon that make decolonization unsettling and
fraught---anxiety-provoking---for those with power and for those without
it but who see no other path beyond current arrangements of
power]{.underline} (Bosworth & Chua, 2021). [Such investments are both
more stubborn and more mediated by *genuine political conflict* than the
*epistemological* mechanisms that Mignolo proposes to
dislodge.]{.underline}

To be sure, I would agree with Mignolo were he to suggest in response
that colonial epistemic logics are clearly at work in the political
situation I have described here. No one has to look far to find the
[*frequent racist representations* of denigrated Indigenous "tradition"
as an obstacle to "progress" taking the form of ever-more
environmentally ruinous fossil fuel extraction]{.underline} (Mignolo,
2010a, p. 326). [Nevertheless, there is no necessary relationship
between the diagnosis of such colonial logics and the aspirations that
drive many modalities of critique]{.underline} (including anticolonial
critique) [to grasp the *possibilities* of *transformation* inherent in
struggles over possible futures]{.underline}. Put bluntly, [forcing open
those alternative possibilities will not come from epistemic
disobedience.]{.underline} [It will]{.underline} more likely [unfold
through impure,"unlikely alliances" that bind together interested
constituencies who come to imagine an "otherwise" to carbon-intensive
colonial capitalism from different entry points. This might
happen]{.underline} by reconfiguring their constitutive identities and
"interests" via mobilization and new forms of solidarity---that is,
*[through politics]{.underline}*.10

Altogether, I have sought to reflect on Mignolo's version of the
decolonial turn by subjecting some of his key interventions to critical
analysis in light of the historiography, intellectual history, and
present-day political stakes of anticolonial thought and practice. As
Rivera Cusicanqui (2020) and Nanibush (2018) both argue, [the
terminology of de/coloniality now takes up an immense amount of space in
debates about colonialism and empire in the humanities and interpretive
social sciences]{.underline}. My arguments suggest that [scholars
invested in anticolonial critique ought to turn to more worldly forms of
anticolonialism]{.underline}. [In this]{.underline} latter [orientation,
the role of epistemology in political struggles is studied both
theoretically and as a question of political organizing and
strategy]{.underline}. What is "decolonizing" here is the effort to
transform those epistemologies as they feed into and naturalize colonial
power relations.

This essay has aimed to probe whether de/coloniality delivers as an
improved mode of critique that patches up the perceived failures of
anticolonial thought to deal with the present-day dilemmas of ongoing
colonization. I have contended that such investments in decoloniality as
an improved analytic of colonial power are often premised on
insufficiently grappling with the complexities of those very histories
of anticolonialism and with the strengths of some existing material
analyses of colonial power, such as scholarship and activism in
Indigenous and settler-colonial studies. These latter investigations
usefully foreground and diagnose what Nanibush (2018, p. 29) aptly
characterizes as "the uneven political field of colonialism."

[Mignolo's de/coloniality is not just *another "option."*]{.underline}
Rather, [**it is a wrong turn altogether**. Efforts to render the past
of decolonization mainly through its pathologies and the present of
decolonization through an epistemic politics *undercuts serious
critical-theoretic reflection on the contested meanings of actual
struggles of and for decolonization today*]{.underline}. [**Insisting on
more worldly approaches to anticolonialism is a better---indeed,
politically urgent---starting point**.]{.underline}

### Perm -- Extinction

#### Perm do both -- the net benefit is [democratic existential risk assessment]{.underline} -- a multitude of perspectives makes democracies more likely to prevent war. 

**Cremer and Kemp**, Cremer is a doctoral student at the Human
Information Processing Lab at the University of Oxford -- Kemp is a
Faculty Fellow at the Notre Dame Institute for Advanced Studies,
**2024**

\[Carla and Luke, An Anthology of Global Risk edited by SJ Beard and Tom
Hobson, Ch 2: Democratising Risk: In Search of a Methodology to Study
Existential Risk, September 2024,
https://www.openbookpublishers.com/books/10.11647/obp.0360, pgs. 110-112
