6\. Democratising Risk

[**There is an intimate and neglected relationship between existential
risk and democracy.** Democracy must be central to efforts to prevent
and mitigate catastrophic risks.]{.underline} It is also an antidote to
many of the problems manifest in the TUA. Do those who study the future
of humanity have good grounds to ignore the visions, desires, and values
of the very people whose future they are trying to protect? Choosing
which risks to take must be a democratic endeavour.

We understand democracy here in accordance with Landemore as the rule of
the cognitively diverse many who are entitled to equal decision-making
power and partake in a democratic procedure that includes both a
deliberative element and one of preference aggregation (such as majority
voting).178 Decision-making procedures are not either democratic or
non-democratic, but instead lie on a spectrum. They can be more or less
democratic, inclusive, and diverse.

We posit three reasons for why **[we should democratise research and
decision-making in existential risk:]{.underline}** [the nature of
collective decision making about human futures, the superiority of
democratic reason, and democratic fail-safe mechanisms.]{.underline}

**[Avoiding human extinction]{.underline}**, [or crafting a desirable
long-term future]{.underline}, **[is a communal project]{.underline}**.
Scholars of existential risk who take an interest in the future of Homo
sapiens are choosing to consider the species in its entirety. If certain
views are excluded, the arguments for doing so must be compelling.

Democracy will improve our judgments in both the governance and the
study of existential risks. [Asking how our actions today influence the
long-term future is one of the most difficult intellectual tasks to
unravel, and if there is a right path, democratic procedures will have
the best shot at finding it.]{.underline} Hong and Page179 demonstrate
both theoretically and computationally that a diverse group of
problem-solving agents will show greater accuracy than a less diverse
group, even if the individual members of the diverse group were each
less accurate. **[Accuracy gains from diversity trump the gains from
improving individual accuracy.]{.underline}** Landemore180 builds on
this work to advance a probabilistic argument that inclusive democracies
will, in expectation, make epistemically superior choices to oligarchies
or even the wise few. This is supported by promising results in
inclusive, deliberative democratic experiments from around the world.181
**[In the long run, democracies should commit fewer mistakes than
alternative decision-making procedures]{.underline}**. **[If this is
true, it should improve the accuracy of research efforts and
decisionmaking]{.underline}**. [We are more likely to make accurate
predictions about the mechanisms of extinction, probable futures, and
risk prevention if the field invites cognitive diversity, builds flat
institutional structures, and avoids conflicts of interest]{.underline}.

There are many ways to consider the interests of the many. Democratic
assemblies could allow global citizens to deliberate about the futures
they prefer, citizens could be surveyed, and the field of ERS itself
could be diversified. At the moment, the field is, as many academic
disciplines are, unrepresentative of humanity at large and variably
homogenous in respect to income, class, ideology, age, ethnicity,
gender, nationality, religion, and professional background. The latter
issue is particularly true of existential risk, which, despite being an
inherently interdisciplinary endeavour, is at the highest levels
dominated by analytic moral philosophers. We need to be vigilant to what
perspectives are not represented in the study of existential risk. **[An
awareness of bias will go some way towards mitigating its negative
effects]{.underline}**. To get close to replicating the cognitive
diversity found among humans, we must begin by inviting different
thinkers with different values and beliefs into the field.

Democracies can limit harms. Any approach to mitigating existential
threats could create response risks, and the TUA seems particularly
vulnerable to this. Despite good intentions and curiosity-driven
research, it could justify violence, dangerous technological
developments, or drastically constrain freedom in favour of (perceived)
security. If we hope to explore ideas but minimise harms, democracies
can be used to moderate the measures taken in response to harmful ideas.
It seems, for example, vanishingly unlikely that a diverse group of
thinkers or even ordinary citizens would entertain the idea of
sacrificing one billion living, breathing beings for an infinitesimal
improvement in reaching an intergalactic techno-utopia. In contrast, the
TUA could recommend this trade-off.

The democratic constraint of extreme measures may simply be a form of
collective self-interest. Voters are unlikely to tolerate global
catastrophic risks (GCRs), which incur the death of a sizeable portion
of the electorate, if they know they themselves could be affected. We
expect that scholars who do not support sacrificing current lives in the
name of abstract calculations, but would still like to explore the use
of expected value theory in existential risk, will be in support of
democratic fail-safe mechanisms.

**[Empirically, this fail-safe mechanism seems to work. Even deeply
imperfect democracies, like the ones we inhabit now, often avert
detrimental outcomes]{.underline}**. **[Democracies prevent famines182
(although not malnutrition).183 They make war --- a significant driver
of GCRs --- less likely.]{.underline}**184 [The inclusion of diverse
preferences in democracies, such as those achieved through women's
suffrage, further decreases the likelihood of violent
conflict.]{.underline}185 Citizens often show a significant risk
aversion in comparison to their government. While surveys are
notoriously difficult to collect and interpret, existing data suggest
that the public has little support for nuclear weapons use,186 but
strong support for action against climate catastrophe.187 We can further
show that when citizens deliberately engage with the subject at hand,
their concern and readiness for action often increases.188 [For example,
citizen assemblies on climate change have recommended widespread
policychanges across sectors, amendments to incentive structures and
laws against ecocide to reach emissions targets]{.underline}.189 Indeed,
many lament that when it comes to genetically modified organisms and
nuclear power, citizens are far too risk-averse.190 The problem is not
that the public is riddled with cognitive biases that make them
unconcerned about global catastrophes.

**[Democratic debate cannot be an afterthought.]{.underline}**
Navigating humanity through crises will involve many value-laden
decisions under deep uncertainty. Democratic procedures can deal with
such hard choices. Greater cognitive diversity should be represented
amongst scholars of ERS. Recommendations on policies that would reduce
risk should be passed through deliberative assemblies and await the
approval of a wider pool of ordinary citizens, as they will be the ones
who will bear this risk. A homogenous group of experts attempting to
directly influence powerful decision-makers is not a fair or safe way of
traversing the precipice.

#### Perm do both -- the net benefit is [resilience:]{.underline} combining philosophy with policy reduces the risk of extinction scenarios AND creates adaptation strategies. 

**Liu et.al.,** Associate Professor at the Centre for European,
Comparative, and Constitutional Legal Studies, University of Copenhagen
,**2024**

\[Hin-Yan Liu, Kristian Lauta, and Matthijs Maas - An Anthology of
Global Risk edited by SJ Beard and Tom Hobson, Ch 4. Governing Boring
Apocalypses: A New Typology of Existential Vulnerabilities and Exposures
for Existential Risk Research, September 2024,
https://www.openbookpublishers.com/books/10.11647/obp.0360, pgs 167-170,
Accessed July 14 2025 \-- HDCSW\]

Contributions and Limitations of Law and Policy Tools for Existential
Risks

[While our deconstruction of existential risks leads to fairly broad
claims, it also provides a few concrete questions and
insights]{.underline}. First and foremost, if existential risks can
indeed be triggered by nonexistential hazards, we need to broaden the
scope of investigation in order to draw a more accurate roadmap of the
existential risks field: one which can deal with questions of
vulnerability and exposure explicitly.

Second, the type of perceived challenge channels the range of
appropriate responses which can be developed. While existential hazards
may appropriately be met by narrower forms of technical solutions and
technologically-oriented mitigation strategies, our broader perspective
of existential risks open up other toolboxes to confront existential
risks. In particular, **[social vulnerability and human driven
(anthropogenic) exposure require improved governance and coordination
for adaptation strategies]{.underline}**. Thus, when we reconstruct
existential hazards through the optics of the social systems' inability
to withstand them they, per definition, become social phenomena. **[As
noted, many existential risk scholars have recently recognised the
importance of reaching out to, and incorporating, law and governance
approaches, even where the origin of the existential hazard itself is
technological]{.underline}**. **[The critical role of such law and
governance approaches should be even more self-evident]{.underline}**
[where the problems in question --- the origins of existential
vulnerability and exposure --- are themselves social, not
technological.]{.underline}

**[This opens up a field for law and governance scholars to work more
productively and on an equal footing with technical experts and
philosophers]{.underline}**. [Moreover, this allows for a different set
of research questions to be posed as to how we might reduce the
vulnerabilities underlying the existential risks against humanity, and
our collective exposure to hazards leading to existential
outcomes]{.underline}. In doing so, our taxonomy has the potential to
elevate relevant aspects of otherwise mundane considerations within
politics, economics and society to the plane of existential risks. In
garnering this attention, we hope that **[law and policy tools might be
more productively incorporated and deployed as a means to building
resilience and robustness]{.underline}**. Here, central legal
institutions as rights, responsibility and societal relations might in
fact contribute substantially to reducing both our vulnerability
towards, and exposure to, existential risks.

The obvious limitations of this approach reside in the observation that
many contemporary existential hazards, vulnerabilities and exposures are
anthropogenic. This raises the spectre of either "iatrogenesis"
("\[complications\] caused by the healer"), where our attempts at
treating a problem accidentally give rise to new, potentially worse
ailments. Thus, in our attempt to curtail existential vulnerabilities
and exposures, we may inadvertently generate new or different
existential risks. Yet, the framing remains critical: the vantage points
created in our proposed taxonomy encourages alternative ways of thinking
about existential risks and provide different accommodation strategies.

[Finally, the perspective provided by existential vulnerabilities might
also foster solutions that will be of more general benefit to humanity
as tangential effects of efforts taken to reducing our collective
vulnerability and exposure to existential risks]{.underline}. While this
appears to be of a lower order of concern at first flush, our taxonomy
appears to bind existential risks together with phenomena occurring at
different levels. In this sense, existential vulnerabilities and
exposures may possess fractal characteristics,82 reflecting the
complexity of their constitution. Support for this claim might reside in
the scalability of hazards and vulnerabilities in particular: if
pedestrian threats can cascade into existential outcomes, for example,
then mundane measures might feedback to reinforce humanity against
existential risks. Pushing this to its limits, it is possible the
seemingly oblique effects of improved governance undertaken to shore up
existential vulnerabilities actually end up as one of the very sources
of humanity's resilience and robustness against existential outcomes.

Concluding Thoughts

The lessons that we can draw from deconstructing existential risks into
hazards, vulnerabilities and exposures can be divided into internal and
external lessons for the field of existential risk research.

In terms of the lessons for existential risk research, our taxonomy
suggests that we may presently reside in a situation of pervasive risk.
In identifying the catalogue of existential hazards looming over
humanity, and focusing attention to confronting these challenges, the
perception is that the outcome of these efforts is a lowering of the
overall probability of an actualised existential risk. If our efforts
are not actually achieving this, however (because they do not address
vulnerabilities or exposures, only direct hazards), we run the risk of
achieving safety that is merely "symbolic": we perceive that we are "all
clear" --- that we have successfully steered humanity past "existential
outcomes" --- when we are in fact all the more fragile. [Defeating a
global pandemic, or securing mankind from nuclear war, would be historic
achievements; but they would be hollow ones if we were to succumb to
social strife or ecosystem collapse decades later]{.underline}. **[By
proposing alternative paths that lead to existential outcomes, our
taxonomy can recalibrate the calculus and reduce the prospect of an
existential outcome.]{.underline}**

Our taxonomy also provides the groundwork for concrete strategies for
meeting the existential challenges revealed by our deconstruction of
existential risks. In essence, our taxonomy enables more productive
cross-disciplinary cooperation amongst researchers from the existential
risk community and various other disciplines, in assessing the dynamics
that might lead towards catastrophic or "existentially adverse"
outcomes.

**[This step in itself seems to enhance resilience and robustness by
fostering greater variety of policy and governance responses ---
responses which can move beyond mitigation alone, to extend to
adaptation, and which can better anticipate the strengths and weaknesses
of governance]{.underline}**. Two key limitations latent within such
approaches need to be acknowledged. First, that these new perspectives
to confronting existential risks import ingrained societal and
institutional problems manifest in lower orders of problems. Second,
that the additional complexity introduced into the field of existential
risks necessarily makes attempts at framing responses more difficult.
The payoffs of such a trade-off are open for discussion.

### Perm -- State Demands Good

#### Permutation -- decolonial advocacy can be combined with state action to produce legislative wins. 

**Barker and Lowman**, Barker is a human geographer (PhD, University of
Leicester, UK, 2013) specialising in settler colonialism, social change
and decolonial activism. \-\-- Lowman is a historian and sociologist
(PhD, Warwick University, UK, 2014) specialising in the settlement of
the Pacific Northwest, **2024**

Adam and Emma, "Ch 19: Indigenous Resurgence", Handbook of Indigenous
Public Policy, EE Publishing, 2024,
https://www.e-elgar.com/shop/usd/handbook-of-indigenous-public-policy
9781800377004.htmlsrsltid=AfmBOopY1hK336j5ed3YfIBuGNMPxi7lpgejC2U2EmnLsyzi6KENt4W4
Pgs. 365-367-- HDCSW -- Accessed July 15 2025\]

LIMITS OF RESURGENCE

[Resurgence is not an uncontested process, but more than opposition from
settler governments and reactionary 'unsettled' settler people, there
are clear critiques of some practices and articulations of
resurgence.]{.underline} Perhaps the clearest and most concise of these
is from Lightfoot (2020), who refers to a 'pessimism trap' in some of
the core scholarly works on resurgence. Lightfoot begins by identifying
an over-reliance and misapplication of Fanonian thought in the works of
three key resurgence scholars: Alfred (2005), Corntassel (2007, 2008)
and Coulthard (2014). [By applying a degree of militantism that
Lightfoot critiques as unwarranted and rejecting any engagement with the
state whether domestically or in the international sphere, **the
horizons of Indigenous resurgence movements are limited and, in fact,
trapped in an endlessly agonistic position with respect to the
state.**]{.underline} Lightfoot also identifies that these works take
hard lines on political 'purity' issues, juxtaposing authentic and
co-opted individuals and movements, ignoring the complex lived realities
of Indigenous People in a settler colonial dominated society. **[By
contrast, Lightfoot offers up]{.underline}** [the dynamic creation of
the United Nations Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous People ('the
Declaration') as being driven by both]{.underline} [**grassroots and
institutional actors, requiring cooperation with** a variety
of]{.underline} **[state powers and]{.underline}** [entities and with
the potential to create significant change for]{.underline}
**[Indigenous communities]{.underline}** [despite the continued
existence of the settler state.]{.underline}

It is important to note here that Lightfoot's critiques are largely the
articulations of scholars who, while influential to many Indigenous
activists, also describe their individual versions and visions of
resurgence. As we have discussed here, resurgence takes place in a
myriad of forms. It can include working through institutions like
universities, many of which have served as extensions of the state to
colonise Indigenous knowledge. The successful carving out of space for
Indigenous thought and resurgence-based education in and around the
established system of higher education and the way that this relies on,
for one example, state funding of Indigenous students' tuitions
demonstrates that the complete rejection of the state and drawing of
hard lines of purity are not actually required for resurgence actions to
function. However, Lightfoot is correct in pointing out that there are
risks of resurgence becoming reified into an unreachable standard of
activism that can, ultimately, badly demotivate many grassroots people
(Bobel, 2007).

In the same way that decolonisation risks being used metaphorically and
expansively and, in so doing, becoming drained of all political and
material meaning (Tuck & Yang, 2012), **[resurgence risks becoming
rigidly defined and inaccessible.]{.underline}** For example, urban
Indigenous communities who cannot easily access territories for
traditional land-based practices may find the discourse alienating. The
same can be said for reconnecting Indigenous People who have been
severed from culture by pro cesses like residential schooling and
out-adoption.9 These Indigenous communities find ways to work creatively
with settler communities outside of the strict confines of the state. In
fact, these Indigenous People come to resurgence through academia, as is
the case for the scholars Lightfoot critiques.

While drawing hard lines on many issues may be counter-productive, one
of the few hard lines that resurgence-based approaches generally agree
on is the need for the political economy of state-supported capitalism
to be eliminated from Indigenous lands. As Coulthard (2013) popularly
articulated in his provocative blog post titled 'For our nations to
live, capitalism must die', capitalism is positioned as 'parasitic' on
Indigenous lands and Peoples.10 Here, Coulthard references settler
colonisation's historical and current role in fuelling global
capitalism. As a postcolonial scholar, Gurminder K. Bhambra (2011) has
said, the development of the 'modern' world--- including the idea of
modernity itself, industrial capital, and global networks of power and
commodities---is the result of the confluences brought about by European
imperialism and colonialism. Colonisation of the Americas, Australia and
elsewhere set in motion consumptive and expansionist settler economies
premised on capitalist ownership of property and exploitation of
'frontier' spaces often in the form of resource extraction, such as the
environmentally destructive production of oil from tar sands in places
like Alberta (Preston, 2013). It is important not to conflate settler
colonialism and capitalism---at the risk of colonial formations
'shapeshifting' to rely on non-capitalist economics while maintaining
settler colonial narratives and structures of ownership and Indigenous
dispossession (Barker, 2021). Nevertheless, it is generally agreed
across articulations of resurgence that capitalism poses a direct threat
to Indigenous lands and lives through environmental destruction and
impoverishment. It also displaces and detracts from traditional
Indigenous economic systems. Returning here to resurgence based on the
revitalisation of traditional food systems, the goal is not simply to
have a sufficient amount of food but to revitalise larger land-based
practices that centre on food, including international trade in
foodstuffs. For example, as Sam, Schmeisser and Hare (2021) state, the
oolichan grease trails--- trails used by nations who caught and
preserved oolichan, or candlefish, to trade the resulting and highly
prized 'oil' or 'grease' with other nations for different goods---
represent more than just transportation routes. Rather, they are also
the material basis for place-knowledge of multiple environments. They
are bound up in histories of international cooperation and trade and
systems of knowledge dissemination in the form of storytelling and oral
history. While it is possible, as Sam et al. (2021) assert, to
revitalise these trade networks with the assistance of digital
technologies (in this case, what they call the Grease Trail Digital
Storytelling Project), resurgence-based analysis suggests that what is
not possible is revitalising these trade networks within a political
economy dominated by capitalism

That said, what could be accurately described as Indigenous resurgence
movements continue to grow and exert an important influence on local,
national and international politics. Indigenous communities are
increasingly turning to land-based activism, even in times of crisis.
For example, the Cheslatta community of the Dakelh Nation, in what is
currently called British Columbia, reoccupied a traditional lake-side
village site in 2020. This was done without permission or help in order
to isolate and protect the community elders from the COVID-19 pandemic.
[**In other contexts, it is possible to draw direct lines between acts
of resurgence and changes to government policy,** especially in relation
to the overlaps between policing and security on the one hand and
Indigenous claims to land and territory on the other]{.underline}.
Consider that [the land reclamation at Ipperwash Park by the Stoney
Point Ojibway community in 1995 led]{.underline} not only
[to]{.underline} the unjust murder of community member Dudley George but
also to [the Ipperwash Inquiry in 2003. This inquiry made over 100
recommendations, including that the governments of Canada and Ontario
apologise to and compensate the Stoney Point community and recognise
that their land was unjustly appropriated over 60 years
prior]{.underline}---a recognition that might never have occurred
without the land occupation. Further, **[the recommendations in that
report]{.underline}** concerning policing Indigenous communities engaged
in reclamations or protests **[were crucial to preventing the escalation
of violence between Ontario Provincial Police (OPP) officers and Six
Nations of the Grand River community members during the occupation of
the Douglas Creek Estates housing development in 2006]{.underline}**.
While these recommendations have not had the blanket effect of
preventing police violence against Indigenous nationhood movements---for
example, the repeated raids on the Wet'suwet'en encampments in the path
of oil and gas pipelines in the interior of British Columbia in
2019---[it is undeniable that Indigenous communities are freer to engage
in land reclamation and nation-building projects than they were
previously.]{.underline} When the range of resurgence activities is
considered, from the educational programs of Dechinta to grassroots
efforts to revitalise wild rice around the Great Lakes to the WWOS
installations and ceremonial renaming of places like PKOLS, the
potential of Indigenous resurgence movements to create social change
should not be ignored.

## 2AC -- Offense

### Extinction Rhetoric good - Generic

#### Extinction analysis is good -- it produces the path for adaptation strategies against existential threats. 

**Liu et.al.,** Associate Professor at the Centre for European,
Comparative, and Constitutional Legal Studies, University of Copenhagen
,**2024**

\[Hin-Yan Liu, Kristian Lauta, and Matthijs Maas - An Anthology of
Global Risk edited by SJ Beard and Tom Hobson, Ch 4. Governing Boring
Apocalypses: A New Typology of Existential Vulnerabilities and Exposures
for Existential Risk Research, September 2024,
https://www.openbookpublishers.com/books/10.11647/obp.0360, pgs 157-162,
Accessed July 14 2025 \-- HDCSW\]

7.1 Ontological vulnerability

**[The category of ontological vulnerability denotes intrinsic
vulnerabilities associated with human existence]{.underline}**. These
include the possibility that we inhabit a computer simulation,56 which
might be terminated or altered at any time. [More conceptual and basic
vulnerabilities --- so fundamental that we often would not even consider
them as such --- include our existence as biological beings that are
dependent]{.underline} (potentially more so than other species such as
tardigrades) on continuous or relatively uninterrupted inputs of energy
and resources (such as food, water, air, light, ...), which renders the
human species one comparatively vulnerable to "extinction" events such
as a supervolcano --- or meteor-induced global winter. On a deeper level
yet, all biochemistry is dependent on the existing laws of physics
within which it evolved, rendering us acutely and terminally vulnerable
to any processes (e.g. vacuum decay) which would profoundly alter these
processes. Biological deterioration due to ageing processes or exterior
damages might also rank amongst these, although that is conditional on
whether or not there exists a physical "hard ceiling" to how far medical
senescence research might extend human lifespans and reduce other
vulnerabilities.

[As **these are background conditions at the frontiers of
epistemology**, we are unlikely to be able to unveil more than a
fraction of these vulnerabilities]{.underline}. Also, as inherent
features of human existence we have limited abilities to act effectively
in this category. Perhaps the most utility we can extract from
delimiting ontological vulnerability is to restrict its reach: in other
words, to leave this as a residual class of vulnerabilities inherent in
existence.

7.2 Vulnerabilities, passive and active; Built, institutional and
cultural

Passive vulnerabilities are characterised by inaction: the
susceptibility to existential outcomes by virtue of failure to take
appropriate measures. Conversely, active vulnerabilities arise in
association with human activities, as by-products or unintended
consequences. Three cross-cutting sub-distinctions can also be made for
both passive and active vulnerabilities: built, cultural, and
institutional. Built vulnerabilities are characterised by our (passive)
failure to put into place relevant solutions or defences to existential
challenges, or by our (active) failure to repair or correct the extant
vulnerabilities in the legacy infrastructures we deploy, or the
path-dependent ways we deploy them --- even if we have such solutions or
repairs at our disposal. Such solutions can in fact include some
interventions proposed by the existential risk research agenda, such as
an asteroid defence programme or the ability to systematically monitor
for supervolcano eruptions;57 they also cover the active existential
risks posed by the technologies which humanity has introduced, but which
go unfixed --- such as architectural deficiencies creating intractable
cybersecurity vulnerabilities in universally used computing chips.
Because of the technical nature of engineered vulnerabilities, some of
these are perhaps closest to the existing (policy) research agenda of
the existential risk community --- and at present some may consider that
law and policy tools have less of a role to play, other than to
coordinate efforts aimed at addressing them.

In contrast, top-down vulnerabilities resulting from suboptimal
direction and coordination are captured by our sub-category of
institutional vulnerability. Here, the line between active and passive
is admittedly thin, where recklessness can be the distinguishing
feature. Active institutional vulnerability may be characterised by
failure to coordinate to address a known risk, such as climate change,
or cyclical global economic meltdown. Passive institutional
vulnerability may then be understood as directional and coordination
failures that limit the scope of knowledge related to existential risks
--- perhaps an implicit "unwillingness to know", which translates in an
unwillingness to fund blue-sky research into charting "unknown
unknowns".58

Cultural vulnerability encompass the bottom-up societal dimensions,
reflecting how certain social practices may affect susceptibility to
existential challenges. Active cultural vulnerabilities include
customary practices that facilitate the spread of pathogens, increasing
susceptibility to pandemics --- for example, integrated commercial
travel networks and interpersonal greeting rituals which encourage
physical proximity or contact. Passive cultural vulnerabilities include
the exclusion or ridicule of existential risks from serious discussion
in public forums (let alone the halls of power). This increases
collective vulnerabilities insofar as the public and policymakers
underrate the prospects for existential risks,59 resulting in further
marginalisation.

7.3 Intended vulnerabilities

[Intended vulnerabilities are those which are created or retained
specifically for that purpose, and within the existing research agenda
are reflected in the premises of the "AI risk" or "Apocalyptic AI"
movement]{.underline}.60 Another salient example can, however, be found
in nuclear force postures which (in the US context) features
centralisation of launch command authority along with a
"launch-on-warning" doctrine that relies on input from fallible early
launch warning systems.61 Together, this gives rise to the catastrophic
risk of an accidental nuclear war.62 Yet, far from incidental, this is
arguably by design. As the theorist Kenneth Boulding once observed: "if
\[deterrence\] were really stable ... it would cease to deter. If the
probability of nuclear weapons going off were zero, they would not deter
anybody".63, 64 The nuclear force knowingly renders itself more
vulnerable to catastrophic accidents --- sacrificing a degree of safety
for the sake of strengthening operational readiness and deterrence. Less
dramatic, similar intentional vulnerabilities could emerge from a state
intelligence service knowingly holding back back-doors or
"zero-day-exploits" which it identifies in critical infrastructure
software, in the hope that this may enable more effective cyberattacks
against rival states at a later state.

7.4 Existential vulnerability: Mitigation and adaptation strategies

**[This taxonomy of vulnerabilities can provide concrete suggestions for
addressing existential risks]{.underline}**. While the categories of
ontological and intended vulnerabilities may seem superfluous, their
treatment as additional classes allow limited resources to be
concentrated into the most tractable areas. Perhaps the main
contribution of this taxonomy is to highlight how existential risks need
not be active and discernible, in the manner of the "hazards" identified
in the field. Instead, many of these risks can be latent, and
slow-moving. [Moreover, this taxonomy aids in understanding how human
activities can impact paths towards "existential outcomes" in several
ways: (1) intent: by directly creating technologies which pose
existential hazards (i.e. emerging technologies such as AI,
nanotechnology and synthetic biology); (2) (negligence) by establishing
complex systems for which failure is unavoidable;65 (3) and by omission,
the failure to take steps to confront existential risks.]{.underline}

Beyond merely refining the sources of existential risks, the
contribution of this taxonomy lies in creating a roadmap for the study
and integration of risks that have not yet received much or consistent
attention in the field of existential risks. In doing so, we emphasise a
number of existential vulnerabilities, such as global dependency upon a
few species of staple crops, or certain types of globalised technologies
(e.g. SCADA-based systems in critical infrastructure) that are not
commonly recognised as sources or failure points of existential risks.

The study of existential "vulnerability" may suggest that adaptation
strategies are preferable to those of mitigation, both because of the
inherent complexity underlying both forms of structural vulnerability
and because adaptation can now occur simultaneously with mitigation.
This is because the vulnerability analysis in effect opens up a parallel
system where other trajectories of existential risks are at play. The
rough equivalence drawn between traditional existential risks with
existential hazards might have the effect of underselling adaptation
strategies: it is illogical to conceive of robustness as a defence
against the apocalypse, after all. Along with efforts to mitigate or
avert existential hazards, however, **[we can now also plan for
adaptation against vulnerabilities]{.underline}**. Thus, adaptation
strategies are not limited to actions undertaken after "the Fall":
instead, **[they may become rational reactions towards limiting
susceptibility to existential risks.]{.underline}** In order to explore
this potential further, we proceed to examine a taxonomy of exposure.

A Taxonomy of Existential 'Exposure'

As a parallel effort to our taxonomy on existential vulnerabilities, we
set out a classification system to differentiate between different forms
of exposure. It is worth recalling at this point that we use exposure to
express the interface between hazards and vulnerabilities --- between
what kills us, and how we die. Both hazards and vulnerabilities in
isolation remain as potentials: exposure is thus a means of actualising
such potential into existential risks.

Such exposure can further be directed towards either the societal or the
natural environment. This is about what is directly at risk: our (human)
society and the common capabilities and support structures preventing
existential risks, or nature and its carrying capacity and resilience to
future shocks. Thus, we assert that devastating results for humankind
can follow from the collapse of both the societal structures we have
built, as well as the natural environments within which these
constructed systems are embedded. **[Again, the distinction allows us to
single out different examples and trajectories to build alternative
strategies for human survival. As is clear from the examples above, it
also draws out lessons for existential outcomes which might not be
immediately evident from an analysis of existential hazards
alone]{.underline}**. For example, when "exposure" is seen from the
perspective of the natural environment on which mankind depends,
pervasive over-fishing and deforestation, combined with trends in
resource demands tracking population growth, may become potentially
hazardous activities with the potential to curtail human development in
the long run,66 even if they do not affect most humans directly in the
short run.

### Extinction Rhetoric Good -- Climate Change

#### Extinction risk assessment for climate change is goodÂ 

**Lewis, et al, Research Scientist with Enviornment and Climate Change
Canada, 2021**

\[Ilona, "Treatment of climate change in extinction risk assessments and
recovery plans for threatened species", Society for Conservation
Biology, April 20th, 2021, access info
\[[[https://conbio.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/csp2.450]{.underline}](https://conbio.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/csp2.450)\]
HDSW-AV-07-14-2025\]Â 

**[Climate change is contributing to substantial impacts on biodiversity
and]{.underline}** is recognized as **[an accelerant of
extinctions]{.underline}** (Scheffers et al., 2016; Urban, 2015). Our
understanding of the consequences of this newer emergent threat is
evolving rapidly; extinction risk assessments need to integrate this
knowledge into formal assessment processes. **[Despite
this]{.underline}**, our **[results indicate that climate change was the
third least identified threat]{.underline}** in Canadian extinction risk
assessments and that for **[nearly half]{.underline}** [(43.5%) **of all
reports climate change was not mentioned at all**]{.underline}. Further,
we found that **[the threat of climate change was inconsistently
assessed over time]{.underline}** and across taxonomic groups. [While
more recent recovery documents increasingly recommended climate-targeted
actions, the majority were indirect actions not specifically aimed at
threat abatement or improving population viability.]{.underline} Given
the pervasive nature and ongoing amplification of this threat its
inclusion in species risk assessments is imperative to improve the
status of climate-threatened species. **[Failure to integrate knowledge
of climate change impacts into formal assessment processes and
subsequent recovery plans could magnify extinctions risks for all
species.]{.underline}** Our results point to the importance of applying
standardized methods, such as the IUCN threat classification scheme
(i.e., threats calculator), in extinction risk assessment processes. We
found a strong positive relationship between the use of the threats
calculator and the probability that climate change was identified as a
threat. **[Application of standardized processes helps to ensure that
decision-making practices, such as listing species for legal protection,
are informed by consistent, repeatable and transparent
criteria]{.underline}** (Mace et al., 2008; Mothes et al., 2020).
Despite the importance of using standardized criteria for threat
classification, it has been typically overlooked (Hayward, 2009; Murray
et al., 2014). Use of standardized threat classifications could reduce
taxonomic bias as species are assessed across a similar set of criteria
in a transparent manner. We did not focus our analysis on whether
interactions between threats were considered, yet accounting for
potential interactions between climate change and non-climate change
driven threats is salient given the potential for synergistic effects
(Foden et al., 2018). Although current guidance recommends that
cumulative impacts be considered when using the threat classification
scheme (COSEWIC, 2019), it is not clear whether this occurs in practice,
and previous work on IUCN Red List assessments suggests threats are
primarily considered independently (Trull et al., 2018).

## 2AC - Ontology

### Generic

#### Flattening DA: Fatalist approaches to understanding the settler colonial paradigm [locks out]{.underline} the possibility of decolonization by '[putting politics to death]{.underline}'. Reifying colonial relations as transhistorical meta-structures [flattens]{.underline} agency and [perpetuates]{.underline} domination. 

**Busbridge, PhD, Honorary Research Associate at La Trobe University,
2017**

\[Rachel, "Israel-Palestine and the Settler Colonial 'Turn': From
Interpretation to Decolonization", Theory, Culture & Society, Vol. 35,
No. 1, 2018, pp. 91--115, https://doi.org/10.1177/0263276416688544
accessed July 14, 2025 HDCSW -- TW\]

Scholars have long reckoned with [the ambivalence of the settler
colonial situation, which is simultaneously colonial and postcolonial,
colonizing and decolonizing]{.underline} (Curthoys, 1999: 288). Given
the generally dreadful Fourth World circumstances facing many indigenous
peoples in settler societies, it could be argued that there is good
reason for such pessimism. The settler colonial paradigm, in this sense,
offers an important caution against celebratory narratives of progress.
Wolfe (1994), it must be recalled, wrote the original articulation of
his thesis precisely against the idea of 'historical rupture' that
dominated in Australia post Mabo, and was thus as much a scholarly
intervention as it was a political challenge to the idea of Australia
having broken with its colonial past. Nonetheless, [the fatalism of the
settler colonial paradigm-- whereby decolonization is by and large put
**beyond the realms of possibility** has seen it come under considerable
critique for reifying settler colonialism as a **transhistorical
meta-structure** where colonial relations of **domination are
inevitable**]{.underline} (Macoun and Strakosch, 2013: 435 ; Snelgrove
et al., 2014: 9). Not only does Wolfe's [ontology erase contingency,
heterogeneity and (crucially) agency]{.underline} (Merlan, 1997; Rowse,
2014), but [its polarized framework effectively '**puts politics to
death**']{.underline} (Svirsky, 2014: 327). In response to such
critiques, Wolfe (2013a: 213) suggests that 'the repudiation of
binarism' may just represent a 'settler perspective'. However, as
Elizabeth Povinelli (1997: 22) has astutely shown, it is in this regard
that [the totalizing logic of Wolfe's structure of invasion rests on a
disciplinary gesture where 'any discussion which does not insist on the
polarity of the \[settler\] colonial project' is assimilationist, worse
still, genocidal in effect if not intent.]{.underline} [Any attempt to
'explore the dialogical or hybrid nature of colonial subjectivity'--
which would entail working beyond the bounds of absolute polarity-- is
disciplined as complicit in the settler colonial project itself, leaving
'the only nonassimilationist position one that adheres strictly and
solely to a critique of \[settler\] state discourse'.]{.underline} [This
gesture not only **disallows the possibility of counter-publics and
strategic alliances**]{.underline} (even limited ones), [but also comes
dangerously close to '**resistance as acquiescence**' insofar as the
settler colonial studies scholar may malign the structures set in play
by settler colonialism, but only from a safe distance unsullied by the
messy ambivalences and contradictions of settler and native
subjectivities and relations.]{.underline} Opposition is thus left as
our only option, but, as we know from critical anti-colonial and
postcolonial scholarship, **[opposition in itself is not
decolonization.]{.underline}**

### AT: Agathangelou

> knowledge as a commodity. Instead, many non-Western cosmogonies view
> the self, community and nature as interdependent parts of a single
> whole, with which their understanding of the relationship between
> knowledge and the natural world, and of the social function of
> knowledge in general, is markedly different (Tickner, 2003: 305).

Such a [claim is]{.underline} **[not only essentialist]{.underline}**,
[but]{.underline} **[politically ambiguous]{.underline}**. [Imperial
authorities]{.underline} [made]{.underline} **[similar]{.underline}**
[claims about non-Western worldviews]{.underline} in order
[to]{.underline} **[justify]{.underline}** [exclusion on
the]{.underline} [grounds that 'natives' were]{.underline} **[ill-adept
for modernity]{.underline}**, [but]{.underline} also [to cast doubt on
the]{.underline} [authenticity of 'Westernised' native
critics]{.underline}. [It is unlikely that Tickner means to
essentialise]{.underline}; [however, the]{.underline}
**[characterisation of the non-Western]{.underline}** [as more attuned
to]{.underline} [communal values and]{.underline} the [natural world
emerges from the]{.underline} **[dialectical impetus]{.underline}** [to
define]{.underline} the [particular non-Western against the particular
Western]{.underline}. The [definition of the Western as]{.underline}
**[rational and universalist]{.underline}** [is]{.underline} also
[constructed, and is]{.underline} **[complicated]{.underline}** [by any
close]{.underline} [inspection of the variety of]{.underline}
communitarian [religious beliefs, anti-modern sentiment and]{.underline}
[counter-Enlightenment philosophy]{.underline} within modern Europe and
the West. On the other hand, [many non-Westerners]{.underline} also
[see]{.underline} **[knowledge as a commodity]{.underline}**.
[Commodification]{.underline} of knowledge [is]{.underline}, therefore,
[not]{.underline} **[explicable]{.underline}** [purely in terms of
ethno-cultural thought systems]{.underline}.

This raises the related point that the [non-Western
alternatives]{.underline} being [defined need not be]{.underline}
**[insular or 'communitarian']{.underline}** [in order to be
essentialisms]{.underline}. The [claim is not that the 'non-Western'
circumscribes]{.underline} **[too narrow]{.underline}** [a section of

[into separate realms]{.underline}, which are [coded according to some

649--650), [alternative sources of]{.underline}
**[cosmopolitanism]{.underline}** (Munro and Shilliam, 2010)
[or]{.underline} **[local humanisms]{.underline}** (Nakano, 2010)
[demonstrate that]{.underline} [political globalism is]{.underline}
**[universal]{.underline}**, [regardless of whether or not its

[progressive or conservative, emancipatory or oppressive political

with the same race and culture only makes sense within a politicised
discourse where the value of the non-Western has been somehow

Then there are [definitions of epistemic difference]{.underline}
**[based on 'lived experience']{.underline}**. [Although an improvement
on]{.underline} **[territorial or raciological]{.underline}** [accounts,
the]{.underline} **[ascription]{.underline}** [of]{.underline} [cultural

subjectivity [can]{.underline} also **[reduce]{.underline}** [groups of
people to]{.underline} **[stereotypes and monolithic value
sets]{.underline}**. This is evident in the work of some scholars who
take Fanon primarily as a source of 'epistemic blackness', without fully
addressing his concerns about racialisation and the geopolitical
dimensions of decolonisation. For example, the philosopher Lewis R.
Gordon writes that 'Fanon's body. . . is a subtext of all his writings.
... Anxiety over embodiment is a dimension of Western civilization
against which Fanon was in constant battle. The [body, he laments,
is]{.underline} [a]{.underline} **[denied presence]{.underline}**, [and
black people are a denied people']{.underline} (Gordon, 2015: 8). [Even
in **as sophisticated**]{.underline} [an]{.underline}
**[analysis]{.underline}** [of Fanon as Gordon's]{.underline}, [there is
a **danger of essentialism**]{.underline} [through]{.underline} the
[association of black identity with a particular way of
thinking]{.underline}. [For Fanon,]{.underline} [black people were not
so much]{.underline} **[universally]{.underline}** ['denied' as
relegated to certain roles within]{.underline} a [social
hierarchy]{.underline} -- the French empire most specifically. Blacks
could be of higher or lower status, but race was the basis for social
relegation, which alienated the subject from a full, dynamic humanity.
For Fanon, [every]{.underline} **[particular experience]{.underline}**
[is an instantiation of the]{.underline} [universal, and]{.underline}
his [analysis]{.underline} of his own experience [is a demand to

**[generalisable]{.underline}** **[perspective]{.underline}** [from

[pay]{.underline} **[attention]{.underline}** [to the social and

[Embodiment arguments are]{.underline} usually [the]{.underline}
**[vehicle]{.underline}** [for Fanon's presence in IR, and are
often]{.underline} [accompanied with the claim that]{.underline}
**[non-Westerners have profoundly different ways of]{.underline}**

presence' of non-Western agency within international order. Anna
**[Agathangelou]{.underline} (**2016) [links]{.underline} **[different

Logics of epistemic coexistence

Fanon's [concerns about]{.underline} **[imperial
coexistence]{.underline}** [continue to have]{.underline}
**[relevance]{.underline}** [in the way that]{.underline}
[anti-Eurocentric scholars]{.underline} **[represent]{.underline}** [the
relationship between Western and non-Western]{.underline} epistemes.
Associations of Western and non-Western with specific traits are
[reinforced by]{.underline} **[scholarly prescriptions of how the two
ought to interact]{.underline}**. Anti-Eurocentrism is, therefore, an
ethos as well as an analytical approach. [Anti-Eurocentric
scholars]{.underline} tend to [make arguments]{.underline} both [in
favour and against peaceful integration]{.underline} of the non-Western
and the Western. [Claims]{.underline} that the [non-Western must make
itself]{.underline} **[autonomous]{.underline}** from the Western (e.g.
Mignolo, 2009), or that non-Western IR must be added as a complement to
Western IR to produce a more 'inclusive and diverse' discipline (e.g.
Acharya, 2014, 2016) both [tend to presuppose]{.underline} that [the
Western and non-Western]{.underline} **[exist 'out there']{.underline}**
[as de-contested and fixed]{.underline} (or, at least, de-contestable
and fixable) [realms of social and political]{.underline}
[inquiry]{.underline}.11 [Each]{.underline} in its own way can
**[reproduce]{.underline}** [imperial coexistence and
recognition]{.underline}, Fanon's 'seat at the master's table' and
Said's orientalist division of the world.

Anti-Eurocentric coexistence reproduces an imperial image of the world
based on a non-West, which is spiritually rich, radically pluralist and
organically socialist, and a West, which is hierarchical, absolutist and
secular. [Anti-Eurocentric scholars]{.underline} sometimes
**[essentialise]{.underline}** [the Western as a political and
philosophical force that is]{.underline} **[chauvinistic at its
root]{.underline}** (e.g. Hobson, 2012; Maldonado-Torres, 2008; Mignolo,
2011). These [approaches]{.underline} [suggest a]{.underline}
**[fundamental antagonism]{.underline}** [between Western and
non-Western, but, in]{.underline} doing so, [produce]{.underline}
**[flattened]{.underline}** [images of each]{.underline}. It then
follows that steps must be taken to rid or purify the non-Western of
embedded Western traits. [Calling this a]{.underline} **[promotion of
coexistence]{.underline}** [may seem counter-intuitive]{.underline}.
[Mignolo]{.underline}, for example, [is certainly]{.underline} **[very
critical]{.underline}** [of the Western, and]{.underline}
[extrapolates]{.underline} from this a professed desire for a different
world. However, [his critique comes]{.underline} [with the]{.underline}
**[assumption]{.underline}** that [the Western cannot or should not be
transformed in any]{.underline} [fundamental way]{.underline}. Rather,
[the Western is]{.underline} **[fixed and essentialised]{.underline}**
[as an object of binary]{.underline} [opposition]{.underline}. New
universalisms of pluralism and horizontality must be derived from
non-Western thought systems, putting the Western in its place and
leaving it alone (although, see Rojas, 2016).

Alternatively, scholars who favour non-Western diversification of social
and political inquiry sometimes argue that purportedly 'non-Western'
traits, such as 'relationality', can be added to 'the Western' in order
to act as a mediating force on 'its' violence. Coexistence here is not
only the prescribed mode of interaction, but it is the contribution that
non-Western culture is making to the discipline. These
[scholars]{.underline} recognise conflict between West and non-West, but
often [downplay or ignore]{.underline} the **[power dynamics and
structural inequalities]{.underline}** [within representations of
Western and non-Western.]{.underline} [Take Ling's]{.underline} Daoist
[dialectics]{.underline}, which, though it makes the valuable offer of
liminal identities between East and West, nevertheless [reinscribes the
divide by]{.underline} **[assigning ownership of relational
conceptions]{.underline}** [to]{.underline} [non-Western thought
systems]{.underline}. Ling writes that Western political 'common sense'
offers only intellectual resources for violence towards difference, not
for recognition and negotiation (Ling, 2013: 12, 15). Strategies for
negotiation -- perhaps for any constructive, mutually affirming
political relations -- must, therefore, be taken from non-Western
thought systems, such as Daoism and the yin/yang. She does not convey
'hegemony, hierarchy, and violence' as endemic to many societies across
space and time, but 'enactments' of the dominant Western picture of the
world. But for its many evils, the Western should not be subsumed, but
balanced by the 'parity, fluidity, and ethics' offered by 'other worlds'
(Ling, 2013: 14). Here, [war and peace become]{.underline}
**[fundamentally questions of attitude]{.underline}**
[towards]{.underline} [cultural or racial difference, not]{.underline}
**[structurally]{.underline}** [determined results of historical
inequality]{.underline} [within and between societies]{.underline}.

Another [potential pitfall]{.underline} of the ethos of coexistence [is
that it elicits an]{.underline} **[open-ended demand]{.underline}** [for
a proliferation of]{.underline} **['different']{.underline}**
[perspectives, removed from the sociohistorical]{.underline} [contexts
wherein]{.underline} these [perspectives]{.underline} **[gain
meaning]{.underline}**. The [non-Western can]{.underline}, thus,
[become]{.underline} [a]{.underline} kind of **[global 'pick and
mix']{.underline}** [for epistemic diversification or institutional
strategy,]{.underline} and the specific, dialectical [relations
that]{.underline} **[elicit representations]{.underline}** [of the
non-Western are]{.underline} **[broadly]{.underline}** [passed
over]{.underline}. Unearthing different perspectives either becomes a
virtue in itself -- in the name of diversification -- or as a means of
retrieving perspectives, which will grant greater 'openness' (Bilgin,
2016: 137--138; e.g. Grovogui, 2009: 138). This is, of course, not to
say that greater openness and diversity would necessarily be
undesirable. However, [without a]{.underline} complementary **[impetus
to assess]{.underline}** these [perspectives for their ability to
produce]{.underline} [or impede a more just society, let alone whether
they are that]{.underline} **['different']{.underline}** [to begin with,
the]{.underline} [politics of pluralisation are obscured]{.underline}
(see Eun, 2019; Paipais, 2016).

There is, thus, a [danger in]{.underline} **[depoliticising
difference]{.underline}** [when making]{.underline} **[absolute virtues
out of diversity and openness]{.underline}**. While calls for diversity
might be politically constructive, [scholars should not
advocate]{.underline} a plurality of [representations of difference
without **rigorous critical analysis** of those
representations]{.underline}. Edward Said, in the same vein as Fanon,
wrote from an analytical perspective, which was highly conscious of
geopolitical imperialism, and thus critical of certain 'non-Western'
perspectives -- e.g. 'nativism' -- which he saw as detrimental to the
political project of decolonisation (1993: 275--278). While he
acknowledged that the non-West could not be reduced to an inferior or
'backwards' reflection of the West, he also did not support the
coexistence of analytical approaches, which reify non-Western difference
(orientalism) and those that treat it as a discursive and material
construct. This is because he knew that, while not the same as political
praxis, academic knowledge has the power to shape the social world it
studies, and can thus reproduce the imperial division of the world and
attendant political agendas.

### Essentialization DA

#### Euro-western conceptions of indigenous ontology [essentializes]{.underline} of indigeneity and [flattens]{.underline} the nuances of '[embodied]{.underline}, [lived]{.underline}, and [practiced]{.underline} life ways'. The liberation of indigenous people from the colonial past [needs]{.underline} to be first and foremost grounded in a [rejection]{.underline} of grand narratives. 

**Solnick, PhD candidate (ESRC-funded), and Singleton, PhD, 2024**

\[Rachel and Aled, "Editorial Introduction: Ontologies Aren't
Essential", Agoriad: A Journal of Spatial Theory, Vol. 1, Article 1.3,
December 4, 2024, https://doi.org/10.18573/agoriad.34 accessed July 8,
2025 HDCSW -- TW\]

[Euro-Western thought has continued to essentialise Indigenous peoples
through an understanding of indigeneity tied to genetics, biology,
ancestry and, of course, territory. This understanding is deeply
contested by Indigenous scholars themselves]{.underline} (TallBear
2013). In this Issue, we follow multiple [Indigenous scholars and
activists in **understanding Indigenous identities to be complex**
social, cultural and political identities, formed in resistance to the
dominant structures of settler colonialism]{.underline} (Coulthard 2014;
Simpson 2017; Tuck & Yang 2012). We capitalise the term Indigenous
throughout this Issue because it is an identity name. It is a
recognition that Indigenous peoples are political communities with the
right to self-determination.

'**[Ontology suggests something essential]{.underline}**', warned our
call for papers. [Discussions at the theory school questioned if it was
possible to name distinct ontologies, identify them, and their capacity
to challenge colonial knowledge production without essentialising;
without creating categories, boundaries and binaries between 'theirs'
and 'ours'.]{.underline} Many of the submissions here problematise these
discussions. The five papers, two book reviews, and an audio arts piece
that constitute this special issue, explore Indigenous [ontologies that
challenge the colonial perspective that essentialises Indigenous
ontologies.]{.underline} Primarily featuring PhD and early career
researchers, the works in this issue stretch into the conflicted space
of academia, in order to use Indigenous thought to directly challenge
and critique academic normative ways of theorising. As Vanessa Watts
suggests to us, **['\[t\]he epistemological-ontological removes the how
and why out of the what]{.underline}** (Watts 2013: 24, original
italics)'. Where [Euro-Western theory persists in defining ontologies as
static descriptions of what 'is', many **Indigenous ontologies cannot be
separated from embodied, lived, and practiced life ways**.]{.underline}
As such, Indigenous ontologies are living; they adapt and transform and
exist in relation (Byrd 2019; Coulthard & Simpson 2016; Hunt 2014).

Agoriad translates as 'openings' and is the title of Hywel Griffiths'
poem, commissioned for this journal. It contains a perfect image for
this issue. He writes: 'and the door that should be opened / has swollen
in its frame' (Griffiths, this issue 1.2: 1). This resonates with
challenges highlighted in this introduction, where [relational and
transformative understandings (opened) are beset by stuck, static,
essential (swollen) understandings.]{.underline} 'Politics is Hard: An
interview with Professor Jenny Pickerill', printed in this issue,
expands in what ways Indigenous thought can aid the transformation of
Euro-Western traditions, moving beyond a recognition of difference and
into an application. The interview, recorded at the 2023 Gregynog Theory
School, also takes us through the anarchic activism which has informed
so much of her work. She illustrates [the patronising stereotype that
exudes from settler-coloniality, through the **prescription of what
Indigenous life ways should look like**.]{.underline} She illustrates
this modality by describing a settler critique of Indigenous use of
modern renewable energies, because this practice, through the settler
gaze, contradicts traditional sacred use of land. Similarly, Pickerill
describes the legislation around hunting Dugong in Queensland,
Australia, which only legalises the use of a spear rather than f
irearms. [This assumption of representation, Pickerill says, '**locks
\[Indigenous people\] into that colonial past**' (Pickerill & Rose, this
issue 1.10: 9) This fundamentally misunderstands that **Indigenous
ontologies are dynamic, place-based and relational**, making assumptions
about ontologies that constrain the community to a particular historic
imagination.]{.underline}

Pickerill's paper in this issue, 'Unsettling Geography: Enacting the
Politics of Indigenous Ontologies', expands on this discussion. The
article emphasises the necessity of integrating Indigenous ontologies
into the discipline of geography, positing that these perspectives
fundamentally challenge Eurocentric frameworks and colonial practices.
While contemporary geography has moved towards more interconnected and
relational understandings, there remains a significant need to
prioritise Indigenous perspectives and confront the lingering effects of
colonialism. Again, this contains [a critique of ontology as
essentialising, as **Indigenous ontologies are characterised as diverse,
context-specific, and inherently complex**.]{.underline} What is
revealed is the necessity of a deeper engagement from geographers that
transcends mere acknowledgement of difference. The title of Pickerill's
paper is itself representative of the dynamic and renewing quality of
Indigenous ontologies in their capacity to unsettle. It highlights the
importance of disrupting traditional power dynamics and the ongoing
impact of settler colonialism. This process is described as both
uncomfortable and transformative, requiring geographers to re-evaluate
how knowledge is produced and shared. [The call is for a more humble and
place-focused approach that acknowledges local struggles and intricate
relationships, **moving away from grand narratives that often
marginalise** Indigenous perspectives.]{.underline} This appreciation
for the power of small-scale action divulges Pickerill's partiality to
the anarchic activism which she discusses at length in her interview.

#### The aff's ontological framings [devolves]{.underline} 'indigeneity' into [static essentialist]{.underline} idea that's [readily co-optable]{.underline} into violent structures of neoliberalism. [Only]{.underline} a rejection of those artificial limitations can we [engender]{.underline} dynamic struggles capable of resisting the colonial state. 

**Solnick, PhD candidate (ESRC-funded), and Singleton, PhD, 2024**

\[Rachel and Aled, "Editorial Introduction: Ontologies Aren't
Essential", Agoriad: A Journal of Spatial Theory, Vol. 1, Article 1.3,
December 4, 2024, https://doi.org/10.18573/agoriad.34 accessed July 8,
2025 HDCSW -- TW\]

While geography's ontological turn re-animated more-than-human agency,
and reduced anthropocentric understandings, Essam's paper highlights a
[continued limitation of ontological framings. That is, ontologies
continue to be associated with the theoretical, and struggle to include
the cosmological.]{.underline} Quoting Sarah Hunt, she reiterates,
**['Indigeneity "is not just an idea,]{.underline}**" [it is alive,
embodied and relational involving humans and nonhumans, land and
place']{.underline} (Essam, this issue 1.6: 4). As has been discussed,
the [use of the term ontological can be limiting, particularly when it
is understood as the 'way things are' -- a presumption that implies **a
static and therefore essentialising understanding** of the
term.]{.underline} [Essam implores us to remember the multiplicity of
ontologies, Indigenous and other. There is not a singular Indigenous
ontology that can be used to decolonise Euro-Western thought;
**ontologies are uniquely place-based, dynamic and
living**.]{.underline}

Matthew Gravlin's paper, 'To Forsake Becoming: Indigenous Ontologies,
Land Defence, and the Resistance at Standing Rock', reflects on the
jeopardy of co-option. Gravlin argues that [the logic of possession is
both necessary for challenging neoliberal infrastructures and considered
incommensurable with Indigenous ontologies.]{.underline} He exposes
throughout the paper how [neoliberalism is capable of mirroring and
absorbing Indigenous practice to suit its purpose. Neoliberalism is
itself an unstable set of ideas, dynamic and flexible to suit its
purpose.]{.underline} Gravlin explores how [Euro-Western knowledge
systems have appropriated and commodified Indigenous ways of being and
particularly explores neoliberalism's retreat from state intervention
favouring governance through decentralised, adaptive
networks.]{.underline} He describes [Indigenous resilience that
'thrive\[s\], not by taking preventative action against threats, but
through exposure and adaptation to them']{.underline} (Gravlin, this
Issue 1.7:7). This troubles the relationship with neoliberalism: [if
resilience requires exposure to threat, then threat is a necessary
partner of resilience, and **neoliberalism becomes a constructive
challenge to Indigeneity.**]{.underline}

What is more, the reverse is discussed through The Standing Rock
protests of 2016. [As Indigenous mobilisations resist and attack
neoliberal infrastructures, 'the same forces absorb Indigenous
assemblages to boost their evolutionary fitness']{.underline} (Gravlin,
this issue 1.7: 10). As such, Gravlin argues, [Indigenous resistance
cannot 'transform Indigenous people's relationship to the colonial
state', nor does it 'challenge colonial power or free-market
capitalism']{.underline} (Gravlin, this Issue 1.7: 11). Instead, the
adaptive capacities of Indigenous peoples are constructed as exemplary
and are co opted as a further tool for creating adaptive and resilient
neoliberal systems.

[Gravlin's contribution argues for '**specific, nation-based
articulations of a possessing, autonomous subject**']{.underline}
(Gravlin, this Issue 1.7: 13) [as a necessary Indigenous perspective for
the task of anti-colonial struggle and particularly in resistance to
neoliberalism.]{.underline} For Gravlin, [the rejection of
possession]{.underline} (because possession only replicates Indigenous
domination) [manifests from a **static understanding of Indigenous
ontologies**. In this way, Indigenous ontologies are **constructed as
'alternatives to modernist scriptures'**, and this carries the risk of
'creating and deepening an essential divide']{.underline} (Gravlin, this
Issue 1.7: 13).

### IR -- Progress Possible

#### [NO ONTOLOGY]{.underline}. "[Green colonialism]{.underline}" is avoided in [instances]{.underline} that IR can emphasize [cultural dimensions]{.underline} in [soft law]{.underline} documents---[Indigenous exception]{.underline} clauses prove.

**Osakada, Chuo University law school professor, 25**

Yuko, "Sustainable development in the Arctic for Indigenous peoples"
Polar Science journal, published 6/2025,
[[https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1873965225000544]{.underline}](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1873965225000544)
, accessed 7/14/2025, HDCSW - JV

[Osakada (2024) examined the changes required to make the green
transition more inclusive and sustainable from an international human
rights perspective]{.underline}. Indigenous peoples have been
challenging the ways in which this transition is taking place, using the
phrase ["green colonialism." Although in many countries, it has become
common to consult affected Indigenous peoples to reach an agreement
before licensing the establishment of green energy facilities, previous
studies revealed that asymmetric power relationship between Indigenous
peoples, sovereign states and business enterprises contributed to
disguised dialogues and/or agreements among them]{.underline}.
Therefore, the meaning of the right to free, prior and informed consent
(FPIC) of Indigenous peoples should be understood correctly from an
international human rights perspective. [The distinction between
Indigenous peoples and local communities under climate regimes was
initially addressed under the UNFCCC and has recently received renewed
attention due to energy policy changes to cope with rapid climate
change. The Montana climate litigation demonstrated the possibility of
harmonizing Indigenous peoples and local communities by clearly
identifying the unique climate-related culture of Indigenous peoples and
avoiding unintentional confrontation or dichotomy, given the difficulty
of making clear distinctions and garnering sympathy from the local
community.]{.underline} Kimura (2024), however, pointed out the
Indigenous peoples themselves may not see the need for harmony. As just
energy transition alone does not automatically reflect the perspective
of Indigenous peoples, green colonialism could easily occur if their
unique rights are not considered. Negishi (2024) provides a
comprehensive exploration of the legal protection of traditional
knowledge of Arctic Indigenous peoples, emphasizing the vital role this
knowledge plays in their cultural and spiritual identity. Multi-level
legal protection of Arctic traditional knowledge was examined. [At the
international level, these include instruments like the ILO
Convention]{.underline} No. 169 and the [UNDRIP, which emphasize
cultural dimensions and self-determination, respectively. Regional legal
frameworks, which are corroborated by soft law documents, are also
included in them. At national level, Canada, Norway, Finland, and Sweden
incorporate traditional knowledge into legislation and judicial
decision. Kobayashi (2024) examined a trade dispute initiated by the
United States in 2023 concerning Mexico's nationwide prohibition on the
use of genetically engineered corn for human consumption. The analysis
focused on the scope of the legal arguments related to the Indigenous
exception clause under Article 32.5 of the US-Mexico-Canada Agreement
(USMCA) of 2018. This clause allows state parties to deviate from other
treaty obligations, if they deem it necessary to fulfil legal
obligations to Indigenous peoples. The author argues that while
balancing values and interest is crucial for mitigating trade disputes,
Indigenous exception clauses should not be used for protectionist
purposes that ultimately hinder Indigenous participation in
international trade.]{.underline}

### Progress Possible

#### Legal progress for indigenous groups [reduces conflicts]{.underline} and protects [rights]{.underline} -- Arctic progress proves. 

**Dorough**, (Inuit-Alaska) earned a PhD in Law from University of
British Columbia, Canada, and a Master of Arts in Law & Diplomacy, The
Fletcher School, Tufts University, **2024**

\[Dalee Sambo, "Ch 20 Quest for equality: redefining Indigenous--state
relationships", Handbook of Indigenous Public Policy, EE Publishing,
2024,
https://www.e-elgar.com/shop/usd/handbook-of-indigenous-public-policy-9781800377004.htmlsrsltid=AfmBOopY1hK336j5ed3YfIBuGNMPxi7lpgejC2U2EmnLsyzi6KENt4W4
Pgs. 380-382 -- HDCSW -- Accessed July 15 2025\]

NEGOTIATION AND DIALOGUE

**[Turning to negotiation and dialogue, two Arctic examples are relevant
for consideration in other parts of the Indigenous world.]{.underline}**
Readers may want to turn their attention to the long history of
redefining Indigenous--state relations to the still-developing
conditions for the exercise of the right of self-determination of
Greenlandic Inuit.

[The Greenland Home Rule Act of 29 November 1978 is probably one of the
best examples of constructive framework legislation accommodating the
rights and aspirations of Indigenous peoples]{.underline}. The former
Greenland Home Rule Government, or Landsstyret, whose ministers are
elected by the Parliament of Greenland, was given extensive authority,
including veto power over development activities. Since 1978, dialogue
and Indigenous-defined agenda setting has contributed to amendments to
the original Act and the design and architecture for the eventual
independence of Greenland through the exercise of their right of
self-determination.

[Although statehood remains a future objective, it is extraordinary that
peaceful dialogue and negotiation with Inuit within the Danish realm has
yielded authority over both domestic and international portfolios that
will contribute to independence--- from ownership and control of lands,
territories and resources to a determining voice in all domestic
affairs, from education to media to municipal governments **to a
significant role in the renegotiation of United States military bases on
Inuit territory.**]{.underline} **[And all of this has been done in the
context of peaceful negotiation and dialogue, in stark contrast to the
armed conflicts typically associated with threats to the territorial
integrity of a nation-state.]{.underline}**

In 2017, in response to one of the TRC's calls to action, the Government
of Canada put in place 'permanent bilateral mechanisms' to identify
'joint priorities, co-develop policy and monitor progress' (Memorandum
of Understanding, 2017). This commitment resulted in the creation of the
Memorandum of Understanding between Canada and the Assembly of First
Nations (2017), Inuit--Crown Partnership Agreement (2017) and
Canada--MÃ©tis Nation Accord (2017).

For Inuit, this permanent bilateral mechanism3 ensures that leadership
from all four comprehensive land claim agreement regions and the
national Inuit organisation have the opportunity for high-level
discussions about their priority issues and potential solutions directly
with the prime minister and their cabinet. These meetings occur on a
regular basis and, thus far, have resulted in substantive responses to
issues and financial needs to ensure the fulfilment of the Government of
Canada's fiduciary obligations to the Inuit as a people. Natan Obed,
President of the national organisation Inuit Tapiriit Kanatami, has
stated: The Inuit--Crown Partnership Committee will play an important
role as we act on the priorities that matter to Inuit and Canadians.
This committee will enhance cooperation between Inuit Tapiriit Kanatami
and the federal government, allowing us to continue renewing the
relationship between Inuit and the Crown in a sustainable and positive
way. (As quoted in Office of the Prime Minister of Canada, 2017, n.p.)

[Further, the Inuit--Crown Partnership Agreement is complimented by a
significant policy intended 'to promote prosperity and support community
and individual wellbeing throughout Inuit Nunangat', consistent with
'respect and support for Inuit self-determination'.]{.underline} The
Inuit Nunangat Policy (2022) attaches to 'all federal departments and
agencies', explicitly providing clear directives on both policy and
funding of concern to Inuit, their communities and their diverse needs.
Comprehensive in nature, in addition to recognition of and respect for
Inuit self-determination, [the policy addresses Inuit approaches in
relation to priorities and inclusion in decision-making, equity, Inuit
language, research, procurement and a process for evaluation of policy
effectiveness.]{.underline} Ultimately, future outcomes should reflect
genuine equity in the status and conditions of social, economic,
cultural and political realms for Inuit throughout Canada.

LEGISLATION

[A 2021 visit to the United States 'govtrack' website revealed 29
current pieces of legislation in support of Native Americans' and Alaska
Native Tribes' collective or individual tribal concerns, ranging from
the Western Tribal Water Infrastructure Act of 2021 to the Urban Indian
Health Parity Act]{.underline}. 4 Legislation is a common pathway for
government policy to be realised but rarely includes the need for
evaluation of whether such policy actions are effective.

Although the governments of the Philippines (Indigenous Peoples Rights
Act 1997) and Cameroon (see, e.g., UN Committee on the Elimination of
Racial Discrimination \[CERD\], 2019; Office of the UN Human Rights
Commissioner, 2022) have enacted legislation and constitutional measures
to acknowledge the specific conditions of Indigenous peoples, both
remain suspect in lack of active, substantive delivery on their word.
Without question, the enactment of the Declaration on the Rights of
Indigenous Peoples Act (S.C. 2021, c. 14)5 by the Government of Canada
reflects a serious objective of implementation by a democracy in this
century. [Much detailed work remains to be done to achieve comprehensive
implementation of this new federal law across diverse Indigenous peoples
of Canada, but one concrete example to align domestic law specifically
with Article 36 of the UN Declaration as initiated by Inuit is to amend
Canada's immigration legislation (Inuit Tapiriit Kanatami briefing note,
2022).]{.underline} Other concrete developments include responses to the
International Decade of Indigenous Languages, a National Council for
Reconciliation, and the establishment of the National Centre for Truth
and Reconciliation at the University of Manitoba.

#### Pessimism about the state is [self-defeatist]{.underline} and [diminishes]{.underline} indigenous agency in demanding political change. 

**Lightfoot**, associate professor in First Nations and Indigenous
Studies and the Department of Political Science, **2020**

\[Sheryl R. Lightfoot, 2020; "Pessimism in International Relations";

**[PessimismÂ TrapÂ 2:Â The State isÂ Unified,
DeliberateÂ andâ¯Unchanging]{.underline}**Â [inâ¯Its
DesireÂ toÂ DispossessÂ IndigenousÂ PeoplesÂ andâ¯GainÂ UnfetteredÂ AccessÂ toâ¯IndigenousÂ LandsÂ andâ¯Resources]{.underline}

In other words,Â colonialismÂ by settler statesÂ is aÂ constant, not a
variable,Â inÂ bothÂ outcomeÂ andÂ intent. Further,Â the state isÂ not only
intentionally colonial, but it is alsoÂ unified in itsÂ desireÂ to co-opt
