military occupation, or imperialism more broadly, remains a
mystery.]{.underline}

There have, of course, been attempts to integrate such considerations
into a DS framework. The decolonial critique that comes out of this
engagement is perhaps best exemplified by Cusicanqui's own research
perspective, which she has described as defying the capitalist global
economy while simultaneously offering a way out of the trappings of
Andean patriarchy. As such, it is a mode of critique that is, as
Macarena Gomez-Barris puts it in The Extractive Zone, 'both rooted
within and disindentified from coloniality and Andean "tradition"'. Such
approaches recognise that indigenous and African-descended peoples in
the Americas are not static representations of an idealised past, as
Mignolo seems to suggest: rather, they are contemporary and active
participants in the remaking of modernity. [The answers to coloniality
aren't to be found in a static form of Otherness or indigeneity. And
even less so when the conception of the Other offered is one which
mimics the essentialist prejudices that DS scholars are, so they claim,
trying to unsettle.]{.underline}

#### Ethno-essentialism DA \-- Epistemic alternatives in decolonial theory risk enabling ethno-essentialism, which fuels nationalist appropriation, and weakens solidarity.

**Zembylas**, Open University of Cyprus, Programme of Educational
Studies, 20**25**

Michalinos, "Decolonial pathways in education: Walter Mignolo, epistemic
delinking, and the risks of ethno-essentialism", Routledge, 01/23/2025
https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/epdf/10.1080/14767724.2025.2459110?needAccess=true,
Accessed 07/15/2025, HDCSW AS

In the preceding sections, I have examined [Mignolo's]{.underline}
framework for decoloniality, particularly his emphasis on epistemic
decolonisation, and discussed critiques that focus on its political and
material implications. This section goes further into the troubling
potential of his [notion of delinking from the West --]{.underline} a
concept which, while intended to challenge Eurocentric frameworks, also
[appeals to certain forms of **right-wing nationalism** that manipulate
its principles for exclusionary agendas]{.underline}. [The appeal lies
in Mignolo's conceptualisation of delinking as a severance from Western
modes of thought, which right-wing nationalist movements interpret as
legitimising their own calls for a 'return' to selectively idealised,
often ethno-nationalist, traditions.]{.underline}

This selective adoption of decolonial concepts underscores an important
critique: [when severance from the West is framed as an absolute goal,
it risks reinforcing exclusivist ideologies that reject global
solidarity and ignore historical interdependencies]{.underline} (Hull
2021). By situating knowledge production outside of an interconnected
historical context[, Mignolo's framework can unintentionally reinforce
nationalist appropriations that reject valuable cross-cultural
influences in favour of insular ideologies.]{.underline} [This
highlights the need for more **nuanced** approaches on
decolonisation]{.underline} in education that address epistemic critique
alongside careful consideration of diverse political landscapes and the
ethical imperatives they raise. My analysis begins with Hull's (2021,
2022) critique of decolonial theory, particularly his examination of
what he terms 'epistemic ethno-nationalism', which he associates with
Mignolo and other decolonial thinkers. Hull's argument focuses on [the
metaphysical interpretation of colonial difference]{.underline} that
[underpins this form of nationalism within decolonial
thought.]{.underline} By revisiting this critique, I aim to explore the
political implications of this perspective for understanding
decoloniality. According to Hull,

[Epistemic ethnonationalism denies that there are universal epistemic
standards by which the truth, coherence or well-foundedness of beliefs
can be assessed]{.underline} . . . \[E\]pistemic ethnonationalism
associates with each 'ethnos' or 'ethnie' a set of beliefs, concepts or
theoretical frames proper to it, such that a member of E1 who embraces
concepts, theories or methods proper to E2, E3 or E4 ipso facto reveals
themselves to be at best confused or brainwashed, at worst a
collaborator or fifth-columnist[. Epistemic
ethnonationalism]{.underline} therefore [denies that achieving advances
in knowledge and in ethical and political values can be a shared global
endeavour.]{.underline} Instead, it insists that each ethnos/-ie must
develop its own truth or knowledge, its own values. (Hull 2022, 135,
original emphasis)

In his analysis, Hull raises important concerns regarding the
intersection of far-right critiques of the 'oppressive' West, as
exemplified by the neo-Traditionalism emerging in Russia, and the
[epistemic decolonisation]{.underline} critique of Western hegemony that
[is grounded in epistemic ethnonationalism.]{.underline} According to
Hull (2022), there are notable similarities between these theoretical
frameworks that pose significant challenges for anticolonial struggles.
By identifying these convergences, Hull highlights the potential risks
of [aligning decoloniality too closely with ethnonationalist and
authoritarian discours]{.underline}es that [may]{.underline} ultimately
[undermine]{.underline} the [broader goals of liberation and
justice.]{.underline} Hull's critique serves as a crucial reminder of
the complexities and nuances inherent in contemporary decolonial
discourse, urging scholars to critically examine the implications of
their theoretical positions.

For example, let us return to Mignolo, who writes the following about
race and gender:

Race and gender are two concepts of Western modernity that make us
believe they 'represent' something that exists. Behind race there is an
implied logic of classification (the logic of coloniality) assuming that
people belong to different races and the markers are blood and skin
colour. Behind gender there is an implied logic of classification
assuming that there are women and men. The classifications shape and
guide our perception of society. However, decolonial gnoseological
assumptions say that names and classifications do not refer to what
there is but frame what we perceive (Mignolo 2021, 85).

As Hull (2022) explains[, Mignolo]{.underline}'s argument extends beyond
a straightforward rejection of traditional categories of race and
gender. He [asserts that all concepts ultimately fail to genuinely
reflect the commonalities and differences present in the real
world.]{.underline} Unlike critiques based solely on biological
evidence, [Mignolo's approach is metaphysical, arguing that concepts are
incapable of accurately referencing objective realit]{.underline}y (Hull
2022). He contends that beliefs can only be deemed valid within specific
cultural or geographical contexts -- essentially, they are valid for
'Westerners' or others within particular group identities (Mignolo 2011;
Mignolo and Walsh 2018). By condemning the 'universalising' tendencies
of Western thought, [Mignolo firmly rejects any notion of convergence
with 'Western' epistemologies]{.underline}, advocating instead for a
focus on localism (Hull 2022).

In particular, [Hull]{.underline} (2022) [suggests]{.underline} that
[neo-Traditionalism]{.underline} (e.g. Dugin's Eurasianism in Russia)
[and decolonial theory]{.underline}, though originating from different
convictions -- one aiming to revive a timeless Tradition and the other
to expose and halt exploitation and oppression -- [arrive at similar
positions.]{.underline} Both frameworks assert that each cultural group
has its own unique values and knowledge systems that are valid only
within that group; this assertion appears respectful of cultural
diversity but risks enforcing conformity to predetermined cultural
values on individuals within those groups, regardless of their personal
beliefs. Hull further argues that [both neo-Traditionalism and
decoloniality reject the idea of objective, universal truth, holding
instead that truth is only valid relative to specific cultural or
evaluative frameworks.]{.underline} Both neo-Traditionalism and
decoloniality criticise 'the West' for centuries of economic
exploitation and the imposition of Western beliefs, values, and rational
standards onto non-Western societies. [Both argue that the dominance of
Western knowledge systems has convinced some non-Western groups to view
these frameworks as universally valid]{.underline}, thus the proper
response is 'epistemic disobedience' to challenge this enduring
coloniality (Hull 2022).

#### Epistemological Decolonization is a dead end. It repeats Mignolo's mistakes, ignores real struggles like land return and sovereignty, and misrepresents political aims of anti-colonial movements.

**Zembylas**, Open University of Cyprus, Programme of Educational
Studies, 20**25**

Michalinos, "Decolonial pathways in education: Walter Mignolo, epistemic
delinking, and the risks of ethno-essentialism", Routledge, 01/23/2025
https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/epdf/10.1080/14767724.2025.2459110?needAccess=true,
Accessed 07/15/2025, HDCSW AS

In summary[, Mignolo's prioritisation of epistemic decolonisation calls
for a structural transformation of how knowledge is recognised and
valued. By advocating for delinking and promoting pluriversality, he
challenges the Eurocentric dominance of knowledge systems and suggests
that true decoloniality must prioritise diverse epistemic frameworks
rather than political or material aims. Mignolo's]{.underline}
decolonial [framework draws a]{.underline} stark [epistemic]{.underline}
and [cultural divide between the West and the non-West]{.underline},
[establishing]{.underline} a binary that presumes [a fundamental
incompatibility between Western and non-Western frameworks of knowledge
and politics]{.underline} (Harruch 2024; Temin 2024). [By framing
decolonial theory as a necessary break or delinking from the dominance
of Western epistemology]{.underline}, Mignolo suggests that societies in
the Global South require unique theoretical approaches that
authentically reflect their histories, cultures, and experiences of
colonialism. This perspective implies that theories originating in the
West inherently fail to serve emancipatory aims in non-Western contexts,
thus privileging localised, alternative frameworks as essential for
decolonial work.

Critiques of Mignolo's decolonial framework

In this section, I aim to analyse critiques of Mignolo's decolonial
framework by identifying three primary pitfalls. [First, Mignolo's
essentialist conception of modernity and the West oversimplifies Western
epistemologies while romanticising non-Western
perspectives.]{.underline} Critics argue that this binary approach risks
caricaturing the West as a homogenous entity responsible for all
colonial structures, while idealising the Global South as the primary
site of resistance and 'authentic' knowledge production, an approach
that can reinforce stereotypes and ignore diversity within each region[.
Second, Mignolo's emphasis on epistemological decolonisation -- which
prioritises the delinking of knowledge systems from 'the West' -- often
overlooks the material and political dimensions of anticolonial
struggles, which traditionally focus on tangible outcomes such as land
reclamation, political representation, and economic justice. This
emphasis on epistemology]{.underline} at times [misidentifies
or]{.underline} even [sidelines the core objectives of anticolonial
thought and politics, which frequently emphasise political liberation
alongside cognitive liberation. Finally, Mignolo's framework has been
critiqued for failing to sufficiently historicise colonial practices and
to account for the specific needs and goals of Indigenous scholars, who
often call for a recognition of their political (e.g. sovereignty) and
material]{.underline} (e.g. land return) [aims over purely epistemic
concerns.]{.underline} Critics suggest that decolonisation should be
grounded in both the historical realities of colonialism and the lived
experiences of Indigenous and marginalised communities, which cannot be
fully addressed by focusing on knowledge production alone. Each of these
critiques is further discussed below.

One of the central criticisms of [Mignolo's]{.underline} approach to
modernity and the West lies in [his portrayal of Western epistemologies
as a singular, monolithic force]{.underline}, which he considers
inherently complicit in colonial domination[. By consolidating a wide
range of ideological traditions -- e.g. conservatism, Christianity,
liberalism, socialism, and even Enlightenment thought -- into a single
tradition and concept]{.underline} assumed to be Western or modern[,
Mignolo overlooks significant distinctions between these traditions and
their complex internal debates]{.underline} (Harruch 2024). For example,
while elements of liberalism have indeed been used to justify colonial
expansion, others have provided a foundation for civil rights movements
and anti-colonial arguments (VÃ¡zquez-Arroyo 2018). Similarly, Marxism
has played a significant role in anti-colonial and liberation struggles,
yet it is also criticised by Mignolo as part of the Western colonial
project (Temin 2024). This unvarying treatment of Western ideologies as
uniformly oppressive 6 M. ZEMBYLAS simplifies the reality of Western
intellectual traditions, ignoring the historical and political tensions
that have, in many cases, fostered resistance against colonial powers
rather than compliance with them.

Moreover, by romanticising non-Western perspectives, Mignolo's framework
risks reinforcing an essentialist view of the Global South, implying
that these societies inherently possess more 'authentic' and liberating
epistemologies (Moosavi 2020; Temin 2024). This framing creates an
idealised dichotomy between the West and non-West, attributing a purity
or innocence to the latter that is historically inaccurate and
politically limiting (Vickers 2020). This binary can obscure the roles
that complex social movements and diverse political ideologies from
within both Western and nonWestern contexts have played in resisting
colonial structures (Harruch 2024). This binary also fails, according to
Harruch, to acknowledge that the Global South has been shaped not only
by resistance to colonialism but also by internal social conflicts and
power dynamics that cannot be explained through a purely anti-Western
lens. Thus, critics argue that [Mignolo's emphasis on the West as the
ultimate locus of coloniality risks perpetuating an uncritical
valorisation of nonWestern epistemologies, rather than fostering a
nuanced understanding of global power dynamics and emancipatory
potential across contexts]{.underline} (Temin 2024).

Another criticism of [Mignolo's]{.underline} framework is that [his
emphasis on epistemological decolonisation often overlooks the material
and political dimensions of anticolonial struggles.]{.underline} As
Temin (2024) writes, 'Mignolo (mis)identifies political decolonisation
exclusively with limited struggles for the political form of national
independence' (5). [In other words, Mignolo critiques anticolonial
struggles for national liberation as having an overly limited conception
of transformation, one focused mainly on the replacement of colonial
rule with native rule by seising control over state
institutions.]{.underline} He suggests that these movements primarily
aim to transfer political power within existing frameworks of state
authority, rather than challenging or dismantling the underlying
structures of coloniality. By framing anticolonialism in this way,
[Mignolo suggests that such struggles often fail to achieve deeper,
epistemic decolonisation,]{.underline} as they continue to rely on
inherited Western notions of statehood, governance, and authority (Temin
2024).

However, as Temin argues, [this perspective is limited, because it fails
to take seriously the complex histories of anticolonialism(s), hence
Mignolo's depiction of 'decolonisation as a narrowly political, entirely
top-down, uncritically Eurocentric,]{.underline} and
modernisation-oriented political telos \[...\] is unpersuasive' (2024,
5). Temin points out further that 'Mignolo's portrayal of the narrow
horizons of anticolonial movements disavows their complexity, conceptual
innovation, and their popular, worldmaking, and critically attuned
dimensions' (2024, 6). [Mignolo's focus on 'the epistemic' diminishes
the contributions of key anticolonial thinkers like Fanon and CÃ©saire,
who view anticolonialism as both an intellectual and political project
that unites diverse practices and experiences of resistance against
colonialism and imperialism worldwide]{.underline} (Zembylas 2024). By
emphasising epistemic decolonisation, Mignolo shifts attention away from
the multifaceted efforts of these thinkers, which involve not only
critique but also concrete, collective action against colonial
structures[. This approach risks sidelining the anticolonial legacies
that have sought to transform oppressive political and social
realities]{.underline} in ways that directly confront imperial power
dynamics across various global contexts.

Finally[, Mignolo's framework has been]{.underline} critiqued [for
insufficiently contextualising colonial practices within their specific
historical timelines and for not adequately addressing the distinct
needs and aims of Indigenous scholars]{.underline} (Temin 2024[). Many
Indigenous scholars]{.underline} (e.g. Coulthard 2014; Simpson 2014;
Tuck and Yang 2012; Wolfe 2006[) emphasise the importance of
acknowledging local cultural and political practices as well as land
rights, which often require tangible political solutions beyond
Mignolo's largely epistemic focus.]{.underline} Critics argue that by
centering the decolonisation of knowledge, [Mignolo's approach risks
overlooking]{.underline} these [concrete priorities that are essential
to Indigenous movements and their visions of self-determination and
sovereignty.]{.underline} In particular, Temin (2024) makes two
important claims regarding Mignolo's approach to colonial power
structures. First, [Temin argues that Mignolo misinterprets forms of
colonial domination that are more accurately understood through
frameworks like settler colonialism and indigeneity, which focus on
ongoing territorial occupation and cultural erasure.]{.underline} Hence,
Temin suggests[, it is misleading to treat these interconnected material
and ideological practices as merely elements or outcomes of the
'coloniality of knowledge'.]{.underline} Second, Mignolo's emphasis on
epistemic decolonisation, rather than a historically situated analysis
of power relations, overlooks how epistemology itself is shaped by
material and ideological power structures (Temin 2024). This lack of
focus on material power relations limits the framework's ability to
fully address the role of colonialism in shaping societal structures and
ideologies. As Temin summarises the political implications of Mignolo's
framework:

**[\[T\]he turn to coloniality as a purely epistemic register of
critique directs significant interpretive attention away from the
crucial fact that settler epistemic and aesthetic disavowal of the
grounds of Indigenous mobilisation and critique are part-and-parcel of
settlers' long-term accumulation of material and intergenerational
wealth through removal and displacement]{.underline}**. (2024, 9)

#### ALT doesn't solve\-\-- Solely focusing on critique, analysis, or a mindset shift decenters the goal of decolonization and makes it impossible to be enacted. 

**GETACHEW,** Professor in the departments of Political Science and
Race, Diaspora and Indigeneity at the University of Chicago, and
MANTENA, Associate Professor of Political Science at Yale University,
**2021**

Adom and author Karuna, "Anticolonialism and the Decolonization of
Political Theory", Critical Times, 12/1/2021, Duke University Press,
pgs. 360- 362, accessed: 07/14/2025 HDCSW- LA

One potent rubric that draws together these various lines of revision is
**[a shared interest in diagnosing the Eurocentric character of the
field and offering remedies for its overcoming]{.underline}**. This
**[arguably is the most prominent way of articulating what it means to
decolonize political theory. This critical move]{.underline}** is
salutary and compelling, and it **[has initiated probing discussions of
the limitations, blind spots, and exclusions of the purportedly
universal theories, categories, and narratives of Western political
thought. It has motivated calls for more inclusion and recognition of
non-European thought, and generated important political and
philosophical debates about how to chart and address the ongoing
legacies of empire and racial domination today]{.underline}**. **[At the
same time, we worry that the overriding concern with Eurocentrism is
indicative of a problem, a persistent limit of these approaches, namely
that they tend to focus on Western political thought as the sole object
of critique and analysis.]{.underline}**

Such a strategy of internal critique is perhaps understandable in
scholarship on empire and political theory, which has focused on
recontextualizing canonical debates and thinkers by elucidating the
imperial context of the formation and foundation of modern political
thought. But the dominance of this framing is also apparent in studies
of non-Western thinkers. **[When anticolonial and postcolonial thinkers
are incorporated and engaged with, their political and theoretical
insights are very often reduced to the critique of European political
thought and practices. In the process we lose track of what arguably was
the primary context and aspiration of anticolonial argument: an attempt
to reconstruct viable political futures in the aftermath of European
domination. Likewise, even as postcolonial theory's critique of European
thought has come to be more generously cited, the alternative concepts
it proposes for the study of postcolonial politics are rarely taken
up.]{.underline}**

**[While the critique of Eurocentrism is noteworthy and important, it
should not exhaust what it means to decolonize political theory. Our
hope is to advance and pluralize the project of decolonizing political
theory by suggesting ways of generating political theory from and for
the non-European world, that is, by imagining political theory of a
truly global reach. To do so, we begin by returning to some key themes
of anticolonial thought to remind us of some of its theoretical projects
and purposes]{.underline}**. Our contention is that **[there is much to
learn from its methods and arguments to aid the project of decolonizing
theory and, in turn, to offer a path toward the rejuvenation of
political theory writ large. We also hope to correct the onesided
reception of anticolonialism as simply a critique of the
West]{.underline}**. **[Indeed it might be useful to think of
anticolonial thought as having two connected aims---one critical and
another reconstructive]{.underline}**. To highlight these aims across
differing political projects, we read together two classic, if often
opposed, figures, Gandhi and Fanon. **[Much of the critical work of
anticolonialism was]{.underline}**, indeed, **[imbricated in revealing
the false, compromised, or corrupted universalisms of Western
civilization and the hypocrisy of its self-understanding as the beacon
of enlightened humanism.]{.underline}** The record of colonial violence,
exploitation, and domination revealed the limits and fragilities of the
West's commitment to freedom and, especially, its capacity to be the
agent of its universal realizability. **[But it is worth remembering
that this critical project, exposing the complicity of Western
universalism with violence, was not just an argument with and for the
West.]{.underline}** As is the case with Gandhi and Fanon, very often it
was an argument with and for their colonized compatriots; it was a mode
of self-critique meant to diagnose and break the collective enthrallment
with the West.

**[As the field of political theory begins to engage more routinely with
anticolonial thought,]{.underline}** **[we should remember the
importance of the specific context or problemspace of anticolonial
argument, a context that shapes and clarifies the nature and purpose of
its theoretical interventions.6 The attunement to context is all too
rare; more often, the critical insights of anticolonialism are
registered as abstract and isolated conclusions.]{.underline}** But the
structure, location, and audience of critique matters. **[For instance,
the anticolonial critique of Eurocentrism concerned decolonizing the
mind; its aim was to undo the ingrained moral psychology and habituated
practices of colonialism through which the colonized reproduced their
own subordination]{.underline}**. Moreover, this critical gesture was
meant to clear the ground and make possible a reconstructive argument.
This was to make possible an alternative universal, whether that
normative ideal was characterized as a truer humanism, a more liberatory
politics, or forms of freedom that did not require the domination of
others. **[What is also distinctively anticolonial in this form of
utopian politics was its situated method]{.underline}**. Anticolonial
thinkers tried to pinpoint the cultural and institutional
sites---immanent to the experience of the colonized---that could
generate new emancipatory futures. Crucially, this was simultaneously an
analytic and normative endeavor.

After delineating what we take to be the distinctive features of
anticolonialism as a genre of political theory, we contrast this to the
ways in which these insights have been taken up in contemporary
political theory. Here we focus on some of the prominent ways critical
theory has tried to address the philosophical challenges of
Eurocentrism. We explore the seminal contributions of Susan BuckMorss,
Amy Allen, and James Tully in this regard, and consider how they reckon
with the problem of empire as they offer new models of universality. We
argue that **[these attempts, while salutary, remain either too abstract
or too self-referential; they often try to correct the problem within
the terms of Western political thought itself. Decolonizing becomes a
form of intellectual self-critique and self-cleansing, which seems to
proceed at some distance from concrete sites of political praxis,
especially sites beyond the West.]{.underline}**

### AT: Decolonization (Violence Required)

#### Binaries DA - Violent confrontation of harmful systems reproduces a system of binarism that is at the core of colonization

**Kotef, Professor of Political Theory in the department of Politics and
International Studies, 25**

\[Hagar, "Cruel Fantasies: Reading Fanon with Lauren Berlant",Political
Theory, 2025, Sage Journals\] 7/14/25- EM\]

Cruel optimism is when the promise of the future can never materialize
(can never bring the good life it promises) *because* it rests on
attachments to the very conditions it seeks to transcend (CO, 227).
[While **decolonization is presumably a complete break from the present
conditions, decolonization-as-violent-replacement** can be seen as
**incapable** of **transcending the logic** of the present:]{.underline}
this is true both for the necessity of violence (which abides by the
conditions of violence imposed by colonization) and in terms of the
political horizon it offers: [If **colonization builds a bifurcated
world**, **decolonization**---in Fanon\'s version---**offers us a future
that reproduces this structure even if it reverts its
content.**]{.underline} The tools---but also the content of the horizon,
of the vision, of the promise---remain the master's (if we are to
momentarily switch from Berlant to Audre Lorde).

The previous can be seen as drawing on Berlant as a *critique* of Fanon
rather than a way of reading him. Furthermore, it can be seen as
reflecting a perspective completely opposite to his, "a settler
perspective," which, for Wolfe, is the only perspective that rejects
binarism (2013, 257). But another reading is possible in this case as
well.

If one accepts my reading of Fanon in section 1, one can further read
Fanon as suggesting that what renders [de**colonization a form of what
we may call "cruel optimism"** is precisely **the adherence to the
elements that in "On Violence" seemed to be at the heart of
decolonization**]{.underline}---[**not just a celebration of violence**,
as I proposed in section 1, but also **the rigid binarism it both
addresses and reproduces**.]{.underline} While "On Violence" opens with
the claim that "decolonization is quite simply the substitution of one
'species' of mankind by another" (WE, 1) and "not in the service of a
higher unity" (WE, 4)---that is, while the chapter begins with a
complete rejection of any form of reconciliation, unity, or sharing the
space of the colony, it ends with a very different tone.

[By the last page of "On Violence" Fanon claims that the "colossal task"
of decolonization is to reintroduce "man into the world, man in his
totality," and **that this task, to which Edward Said referred as the
reintroduction of "real humanism**"]{.underline} (1994, 269), "will be
achieved with the crucial help of the European masses" (WE, 62). [This
possibility, if not a necessity, of a joint struggle for liberation, as
well as the risks of following a Manichaean logic, are examined more
systematically]{.underline} in chapter 2 of *Wretched*. There,
**[nationalism, whilst seen as a necessary step on the way to
liberation, is criticized for *reproducing* the logic of binarism, which
ultimately reproduces
oppression.[^23^](https://journals.sagepub.com/reader/content/197cb3a0f16/10.1177/00905917251341906/format/epub/EPUB/xhtml/index.xhtml?hmac=1752525688-cK%2B3j6dVDtjaTIb2BRbOIrHwbfG5G9Hp%2FfoVLCuiTb4%3D#fn23-00905917251341906)]{.underline}**

#### Decolonization reflects cruel optimism without endorsing existing forms of colonization \-\-- must be rejected.

**Kotef, Professor of Political Theory in the Department of Politics and
International Studies at SOAS, University of London, 2025**

\[Hagar, "Cruel Fantasies: Reading Fanon with Lauren Berlant", Political
Theory, 07/01/2025, https://doi.org/10.1177/00905917251341906, pgs 3-4,
accessed: 7/14/2025 HDSCW- LA\]

Several clarifications are due before we begin. **[To read
decolonization as cruel optimism by no means endorses the status quo of
colonization. It is not a "sit and wait" kind of
critique.]{.underline}** **[Thinking with Berlant is always thinking
critically about our present. Berlant was probably one of the most
astute critics of the present conditions, of the getting-used-to these
conditions, of the deferred future that prevents real change. But they
were also critical of political promises that are bound to fail us. My
aim here is therefore not to point to the inevitability of the colonial
situation but to try and think beyond its impasses---impasses
that]{.underline}**, I will argue, **[the Fanonian version of
decolonization-as-violent-replacement shares rather than
overcomes.]{.underline}**

A similar clarification should be made regarding the status of
**[Fanon's notion of decolonization as a fantasy]{.underline}**, which
will be integral to my reading of it as **[a form of cruel optimism. If
"fantasy is the means by which people hoard idealizing theories and
tableaux about how they and the world 'add up to
something]{.underline}**'" (CO, 2), **[then the frames through which
meaning is given to peoples' relations to each other and the world are
often far from being rational or calculated. To call something "a
fantasy"]{.underline}** is therefore not to dismiss it as irrelevant to
politics. It **[is to ask differently about its political
doings]{.underline}**. Moreover, Berlant saw such fantasies as necessary
given a reality (a world) that has become unbearable. Fantasies are
often the means through which we can continue being in the world despite
and amidst the continuous betrayals of the world in its promises.
Fantasy is thus the defense that makes life possible. But this means
that how we see the world and give it meaning, as well as how we
construct our relations to others and how we inhabit these relations,
reside somewhere between the real and the imagined. This space, for
Berlant, is an important political space; it is often a collective space
and not a psychic or individual one. Nevertheless, this understanding
also calls us to ask what happens if the imaginary turns into a reality
or what happens when the imaginary makes some realities impossible. For
Berlant, **[there seem to be two possibilities: either the realization
of these fantasies "is discovered . . . to be impossible, sheer fantasy,
or \[else it is discovered to be\] too possible, and
toxic]{.underline}**" (CO, 24). In other words, **[whilst necessary and
often enabling, there is something about these fantasies' meeting-point
with reality that renders them potentially destructive.]{.underline}**

#### There is no alternative, violent [decolonization]{.underline} is cruel optimism: it binds the political struggle to [dead-ends]{.underline} even as it liberates and continues to push [settler and native binarie]{.underline}s that stick with us. Even if violence is key, the alternative produces a new world that's [completely broken]{.underline}, where conditions of life are [compromised]{.underline}.

**Kotef, SOAS University of London, 25**

\[Hagar, "Cruel Fantasies: Reading Fanon with Lauren Berlant",
SageJournals, July 1,
https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/full/10.1177/00905917251341906,
7/15/25, HDCSW - JH\]

[There are two facets to the necessity of armed violence in
Fanon]{.underline}. The first is a factual calculous of gains and
losses. "[Colonialism only loses its hold when the knife is at its
throat]{.underline}," Fanon stated (WE, 23), probably accurately so.
[Colonization]{.underline}---historically one of the most profitable
enterprises of the West---[must become pricy for it to end,]{.underline}
and Fanon offered a good account of this calculous to which I shall
return. But there is [another element that renders decolonization
violent]{.underline} not as a historical fact but by definition:
Decolonization is, [according to Fanon, "quite simply the substitution
of one 'species' of mankind by another]{.underline}." It is an act of
replacement that is "unconditional, absolute, total, and seamless" (WE,
1), [a complete removal in which the colonized demands "not the status
of the colonist, but his place"]{.underline} (WE, 23). As such,
decolonization is a form of violence. In this section, I propose that
for Fanon, **[violence binds the political struggle to dead-ends even as
it liberates]{.underline}**, that according to him, it has significant
phantasmatic elements, [and that what appears at the beginning of
Wretched to be the only possible solution to the colonial situation
**slowly emerges as the destruction of all political
possibilities**.]{.underline} In short, I want to read Fanon as seeing
[this specific mode of decolonization as a form of cruel
optimism.]{.underline} "Violence" develops alongside several moments (in
the Hegelian sense) in Wretched. In the first, [it is explicitly a
fantasy, a dream---"Dreams of possession"; dreams "of sitting at the
colonist's table and sleeping in his bed, preferably with his
wife"]{.underline} (WE, 5) (which [may be dreams of temptation as part
of the fantasy of replacement, or dreams of rape]{.underline}). The last
set of dreams was probably what Arendt had in mind when she wrote: "Who
has ever doubted that \[. . .\] [the oppressed 'dream at least once a
day of setting' themselves up in the oppressor's place]{.underline}. . .
. [\[However,\]]{.underline} the point . . . is [that dreams never come
true." And when they do]{.underline}, Arendt continued, th[ey are
**turned "into nightmares for everybody.**]{.underline} In no case, as
far as I know, was the force of these 'volcanic' outbursts . . . 'equal
to that of the pressure put on them.'" Therefore, she concluded, "[to
identify the national liberation movements with such outbursts is to
prophesy their doom]{.underline}" (Arendt 1970, 20--21). Arendt's
critique here is not a moral critique of the fantasy itself. It makes
sense that, after and against the backdrop of so long and so radical
oppression, the oppressed want to see the oppressor disappear. The
critique is rather [a political critique of the attempt to actualize the
dream]{.underline}. It was a critique Arendt wrote against Fanon, 6 yet
once we move beyond "On Violence" into other chapters of Wretched,
Fanon's claims begin to resemble hers. Violent decolonization
[becomes]{.underline}, in Fanon himself, [either a "sheer fantasy" that
can never be actualized "or too possible, and toxic]{.underline}" (to
return to Berlant). Let us track, then, [the trajectory from fantasy to
realization.]{.underline} The most embodied, most detailed descriptions
of violence in "On Violence" [seem to be allocated to the phantasmatic
world:]{.underline} It is [in the dreams]{.underline}, the dances,
[which are "muscular org\[ies\]]{.underline} during which the most
brutal aggressiveness [and impulsive violence are
channeled]{.underline}, transformed, and spirited away" (WE, 19),
[wherein "symbolic killings,]{.underline} figurative cavalcades, [and
imagined multiple murders, everything has to come out]{.underline}" (WE,
20). It is in the ceremonies, the myths, the poems, wherein violence
appears most viscerally and murder is depicted in the most concrete
details. It is an image Fanon tells us, with clear, yet phantasmatic
presence [in the "imagination of every colonized subject]{.underline}"
(WE, 6), in "[dreams of aggressive vitalit]{.underline}y" (WE, 15).
[Some of these fantasies are clearly fantasies of
decolonization]{.underline}, fantasies [in which the colonized can
"**blow the colonial world to smithereen**]{.underline}" (WE, 6). Others
are fantasies that serve to deflect or transfer both the form of
oppression and the object of resistance (from colonization to something
else): "vampirism, possession by djinns, by zombies, and by Legba, the
illustrious god of voodoo." [These fantasies play "a key regulating role
in ensuring the stability of the colonized world]{.underline}": Since
"the mythical structures contain far more terrifying adversaries," "the
problem now is not whether to fall in line with the armor-plated world
of colonialism, but to think twice before urinating, spitting, or going
out in the dark." In other words, "[it is evident that everything is
reduced to a permanent confrontation at the level of
phantasy]{.underline}" (WE, 20, 19). [These forms of phantasmatic
transference of violence]{.underline} thus **[hinder political
organization,]{.underline}** much [like the aggression of one colonized
toward the other]{.underline}--- that Fanon also identifies as central
to the pre-decolonization stage. Moreover, [many of them provide an
outlet for violence]{.underline} that, as such, [delays its articulation
as political resistance]{.underline}. [On their way to the dancing
scene]{.underline}, for example, "these [men and women were stamping
impatiently]{.underline}, their nerves 'on edge.' [On the way back, the
village returns to serenity]{.underline}, peace, and stillness"(WE, 20),
[since, as we saw, in the dance "the most brutal aggressiveness and
impulsive violence are channelled]{.underline}, transformed, and
spirited away" (WE, 19). We can therefore read **[some of these
fantasies as external to, and even contradictory to, the development of
decolonization]{.underline}**. Yet such are precisely the fantasies of
"the good life" under capitalism of which Berlant writes: **[they bind
us to an image of the world, and of ourselves in the world, that
reproduces the impasses we inhabit rather than allowing us to overcome
them]{.underline}**. [Some fantasies serve as a defense]{.underline}
(CO, 49) [by enabling avoidance]{.underline} (much like those zombie
fantasies); [others by projecting the logic of the present into the
future]{.underline} (like replacement fantasies, a point to which I
return in the next section). [Both are part of the logic of cruel
optimism]{.underline}. Either way, for now, violence remains if not
completely phantasmatic, at the very least sublimated, specifically in
muscular tension, at times spasms (WE, 19), with ideas and concepts that
are being destroyed (WE, 11) and [knives that can be "close to hand" but
not used]{.underline} (WE, 8). [There is blood, but it is "in the
air"]{.underline} (WE, 29); there is an atmosphere of violence. In its
next moment, [this fantasy is realized, and violence becomes
actualized]{.underline}. "After years of unreality, after wallowing in
the most extraordinary phantasms, [the colonized subject, machine gun at
the ready, finally confronts the only force which challenges his very
being: colonialism]{.underline}" (WE, 20). At this point, "the colonized
subject discovers reality and transforms it through his praxis, his
deployment of violence and his agenda for liberation" (ibid.). [The
violence that previously remained abstract, symbolic, or actualized only
in transference can now "change directions]{.underline}" (WE, 21): from
attacking/fearing zombies or releasing aggression in dance, [to
political resistance and thus, also, from fantasy to
actualization]{.underline}. This actualization, Fanon emphasizes, is
essential to the formation of both subject and nation. "The armed
struggle" is formative both to the individual and the nation; in fact,
it is formative to the individual (also) "since each individual
represents a violent link in the great chain, in the almighty body of
violence rearing up in reaction to the primary violence of the
colonizer" (WE, 50); it "mobilizes the people, i.e., it pitches them in
a single direction" (ibid)---which is how, we may argue, sovereignty
emerges.7 (We will later see that for Berlant, [such frictionless
togetherness is never an option]{.underline}, but let us stay with the
actualization of violence for now). This actualization [also **generates
a spiral of violence where more violence on one side brings more
violence on the other**]{.underline}. From the colonized side we find
knives, shotguns, and people fighting with their bare hands (WE, 26,
38), engaging in acts "that have been decreed heinous by the
metropolitan press and public opinion" (24--25), working "towards the
death of the colonist" (44). [The violence of colonization is more
brutal]{.underline}, and importantly, it has been there from the
beginning in its most realistic, physical form: with rifles, tanks,
naval vessels, planes, napalm, and bombs (4, 25, 47), [with famine that
is used as a weapon, including **starving the colonized population** to
death]{.underline} (9, 15), **[sexual violence]{.underline}** (15, 50),
**[beating the colonized "day in and day out]{.underline}**" (17),
**[electrifying his genitals]{.underline}**, and using other forms of
torture (22, 50). In the final moment, by the end of Wretched, [the
devastating outcomes of the realization of the fantasy become
clear]{.underline}. In the last chapter, [violence stays concrete and
specific; it is the violence of the raping of a wife while her husband
joins the guerrilla struggle, of a massacre of twenty Algerian men from
which one survived after being shot in the limb and fainting, of
torture. It is]{.underline} not the phantasmatic orgy of violence from
the first moment, nor the actual, but often [the less-specific violent
struggle of the second moment]{.underline}. It is what happened to or
was acted by Fanon's patients during this struggle. At that moment, when
Fanon looks at the level of the human, [when more humanity is therefore
inserted into the picture, **violence is no longer celebrated. It cannot
be celebrated when it is felt so close, so directly**]{.underline}. At
that moment, it is also no longer altogether clear what its ethical,
political, or psychological value is. [It is certainly not something to
find "exhilarating]{.underline}" (Dean 2024[) but a tragedy]{.underline}
(Gordon 1995, 70ff) [from **which we will never fully heal**, even if it
may have been necessary to overcome the colonial
oppression]{.underline}.8 If in the first chapter, [Fanon can be read as
celebrating violence as the way through which the colonized creates
himself as a man and a nation comes into being]{.underline},[9 then in
the last chapter, we find that **if violence produced a new world, a new
nation, and a new subject as "On Violence" argues, those are completely
broken ones**]{.underline}. "Too possible, and toxic," to return once
more to Berlant; a (re-)creation of a world in which thriving remains
impossible. [The violence of decolonization is]{.underline}, therefore,
simultaneously a necessity and [something that leads to a complete
shattering of both subject and world]{.underline}. Rather than
delivering its promises of a better future, [it ends up reproducing the
compromised conditions of the present or, at best, **leading to another
present in which the conditions of life are as
compromised**]{.underline}. It is, in this sense, a form of cruel
optimism. \*\*\* It may be [that this is an inevitable
tragedy]{.underline}: from the moment the colonial relations have been
established and entrenched, [there is no way to release their grip but
through violence, and once the violence of resistance begins, it is
bound to be met with violence from the colonial forces and thus
escalate. Eventually, it therefore destroys the very world and subject
it also builds. And **yet it has no alternative: any failure to resist
will mean the continuation of the violence of
colonization**,]{.underline} which, in some respects at least, is
worse.10 It may be that---as many indeed argued in regard to Berlant's
cruel optimism (e.g., Wilder 2017)---all we are left with are impasses.
